Fact	HEADNOTE: The appellant was the Chief Minister of Maharashtra between June 9, 198 0 and January 12, 1982, when he resigned that office in deference to the judgment of <ORG> in a writ petition filed against him, but continued as an MLA.
Fact	On August 9, 1982, respondent No.
Fact	1, a member of a political party filed a complaint before a Special Judge against the appellant and others for offences under ss.
Fact	161 and 165 of the Indian Penal Code and s. 5 of the Criminal Law Amendment Act, 1952 and also under ss.
Fact	384 and 420 read with ss.
Fact	109 and 120B of the Indian Penal Code.
Fact	The Special Judge issued process to the appellant.
Fact	Later, the Special Judge over-ruled the objection of the appellant to take cognizance of the offences on a private complaint, and to issue process, in the absence of notification under s. 7(2) of the Criminal Law Amendment Act, 1952, specifying as to which of the three special Judges of the area should try such cases.
Fact	Against this, the appellant filed a revision application in the <ORG>, which dismissed it subsequently.
RulingByLowerCourt	The appellant's Special Leave Petition against this  was dismissed by <ORG> which held that the complaint filed by respondent No.
RulingByLowerCourt	1 was clearly maintainable and cognizance was properly taken of it.
RulingByLowerCourt	During the pendency of the revision application in <ORG>, <ORG> notified the Special Judge to try the off- 3 ences specified under s. 6(1) of the Act and appointed another Special Judge, who discharged the appellant, holding that a member of <ORG> was a public servant and there was no valid sanction for prosecuting the appellant.
Fact	Against this order of discharge.
Fact	respondent No.
Fact	1 filed a Criminal Revision Application in <ORG>, which was subsequently withdrawn to this <ORG>.
Fact	On an appeal filed by respondent No.
Fact	1 directly under Article 136 of the Constitution against the order of discharge, <ORG> held on 16.2.1984, that a member of <ORG> was not a public servant, and set aside the order of the Special judge.
Fact	The <ORG> observed that though nearly 2 1/2 years had rolled by sin ce prosecution against the accused, who was Chief Minister of a State, was launched and his character and integrity came under cloud, the case had not moved an inch further and that an expeditious trial was primarily in the interest of the accused and mandate of Article 21.
RulingByLowerCourt	It further observed that expeditious disposal of a criminal case was in the interest of both the prosecution and the accused.
RulingByLowerCourt	It, therefore, suo motu withdrew this special case and another one filed against the appellant by another person and transferred them to <ORG>, with the request to the Chief Justice to assign these two cases to a sitting Judge of <ORG>, who should proceed to expeditiously dispose of the cases, preferably by holding trial from day to day.
RulingByLowerCourt	Pursuant to the directions of this <ORG> dated February 16, 1984 the Chief Justice of <ORG> assigned the cases to one of the Judges of that <ORG>.
RulingByLowerCourt	The appellant appeared before him and raised an objection that the case could be tried only by a Special Judg e appointed by the Government under the 1952 Act.
RulingByLowerCourt	The Judge rejected this and other objections holding that he was bound by the order of <ORG> .
RulingByLowerCourt	Special Leave Petitions as well as a writ petition filed by the appellant against the aforesaid decision were dismissed by this <ORG> on April 17, 1984, holding that the Judge was perfectly justified, and indeed it was his duty to follow the decision of this <ORG> which was binding on him.
RulingByLowerCourt	It also observed that the writ petition challenging the validity of the order and judgment of this <ORG> as nullity or otherwise could not be entertained, and that the dismissal of the writ petition would not prejudice the petitioner's right to approach this <ORG>, with an appropriate review petition or any other application, which he may be entitled to in law.
RulingByLowerCourt	4 Thereafter, the cases were transferred to another Special Judge, who framed 21 charges and declined to frame 22 other charges proposed by respondent No.
RulingByLowerCourt	1.
RulingByLowerCourt	This <ORG> allowed respondent No.1`s appeal by special  leave except in regard to three draft charges under s. 384 , and requested <ORG> to nominate another Judge to try the cases.
RulingByLowerCourt	The Judge, to whom the cases were transferred, framed 79 charges against the appellant, and refused to proceed against the other named conspirators.
Fact	Against the aforesaid order, the appellant filed a Special Leave Petition before this Court questioning the jurisdiction of the Special Judge to try the case in violation of the appellant's fundamental rights conferred by Articles 14 and 21 and the provisions of the Criminal Law Amendment Act of 1952.
Fact	The appellant also filed a Special Leave Petition against the decision of the Judge, holding that none of the 79 charges framed against the accused required sanction under s. 197(1) of the Cr.
Fact	P.C., and a writ petition challenging a portion of s. 197(1) as ultra vires Articles 14 and 21 of the Constitution.
Fact	This Court granted special leave in the Special Leave Petition questioning the jurisdiction of the Special Judge to try the case  and stayed further proceedings in <ORG>.
Fact	It also issued notice in the other Special Leave Petition and the writ petition, and directed these to be tagged on to the appeal.
Fact	An application filed by respondent No.
RulingByLowerCourt	1 for revocation of the Special Leave was dismissed and the appeal was referred to a Bench of seven Judges.
RulingByLowerCourt	The other Special Leave Petition and the writ petition were delinked, to be heard after the disposal of the appeal.
RulingByLowerCourt	In the appeal, two questions arose, namely, (1) whether the directions given by this <ORG> on 16th February, 1984, withdrawing the special cases pending in the <ORG> of Special Judge and transferring the same to <ORG> with the request to the Chief Justice to assign these cases to a sitting Judge of that High <ORG> in breach of s. 7(1) of the Criminal Law Amendment Act, 1952 which mandated that the offences, as in this case, should be tried only by a Special Judge, thereby denying at least one right of appeal to the appellant was violative of Articles 14 and 21 of  the Constitution and whether such direction were at all valid or legal and (2) if such directions were not at all valid or legal in view of the <ORG>'s order of April 17, 1984, whether the present 5 appeal was sustainable or the grounds therein justiciable in these proceedings.
RulingByLowerCourt	In other words, whether the said directions in a proceeding inter parties were binding even if bad in law or violative of Articles 14 and 21 of the Constitution and as such, immune from correction by this <ORG> even though they caused prejudice and injury.
RulingByLowerCourt	Allowing the appeal, and setting aside and quashing all the proceedings subsequent to the directions of the <ORG> on 16.2.1984 and directing that the trial should proceed in accordance with law, i.e.
RulingByLowerCourt	Criminal Law Amendment Act , 1952.
Fact	^ HELD: Majority: <NAME>, <NAME> and <NAME>, JJ.
Fact	Per <NAME>.
Fact	J: 1.
Fact	Section 7(1) of the Criminal Law Amendment Act, 1952 creates a condition which is sine qua non for the trial of offences under s. 6(1) of the said Act.
RulingByLowerCourt	The cond ition is that notwithstanding anything contained in the Code of Criminal Procedure or any other law, the said offences shall be triable by Special Judges only.
RulingByLowerCourt	The offences specified under s. 6(1) of the 1952 Act are those punishable under ss.
RulingByLowerCourt	161 , 162 , 163 , 164 and 165A of the Indian Penal Code and s. 5 of the Prevention of Corruption Act, 1947.

RulingByLowerCourt	This <ORG>, by its directions could not confer jurisdiction on the High <ORG> to try any case, when it did not possess such jurisdiction under the scheme of the 1952 Act.

RulingByPresentCourt	No <ORG>, whether superior o r inferior or both combined can enlarge the jurisdiction of the <ORG> or divest a person of his rights of revision and appeal.

RatioOfTheDecision	The maxim Actus Curiae Neminem Gravabid had no application to conscious conclusions reached in a judicial decision.

Argument	The contention that has been canvassed before us was that save as provided in sub-section (1) of section 9 of the Code the provisions thereof corresponding to section 9(1) of the Criminal Procedure Code, 1898) shall so far as they are not inconsistent with the Act apply to the proceedings before the Special Judge and for purposes of the said provisions the <ORG> of the Special Judge shall be deemed to be a <ORG> of Session trying cases without a jury or  without the aid of assessors and the person conducting the prosecution before a Special Judge shall be deemed to be a public prosecutor.

Argument	The only question with which we are concerned in this appeal is, whether the case which is triable under the 1952 Act only by a Special Judge appointed under section 6 of the said Act could be transferred to <ORG> for trial by itself or by this <ORG> to <ORG> for trial by it.
Argument	Section 406 of the Code deals with transfer of criminal cases and provides power to this <ORG> to transfer cases and appeals whenever it is made to appear to this <ORG> that an order under this section is expedient for the ends of justice.

Argument	Section 7 of the 1952 Act deals with cases triable by Special Judges.

RulingByLowerCourt	and no procedure which will deny the equal protection of law can be invented and any order passed by this <ORG> which will deny equal protection of laws would be an order which is void by virtue of Article 13(2) of the Constitution.

RatioOfTheDecision	Section 7(1) of the 1952 Act creates a condition which is sine qua non for the trial of offences under section 6(1) of the said Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	The condition is that notwithstanding anything contained in the Code o f Criminal Procedure or any other law, the said offences shall be triable by Special Judges only.
RatioOfTheDecision	(Emphasis supplied).

RatioOfTheDecision	It will produce an intolerable state of affairs.
RatioOfTheDecision	This Court sought to recognise the distinction between finality of judicial orders qua the parties and the reviewability for application to other cases.
RatioOfTheDecision	Between the parties even a wrong decision can operate as res judicata.
RatioOfTheDecision	The doctrine of res judicata is applicable even to criminal trials, it was urged.
RatioOfTheDecision	Reliance was placed on <CITATION_1287844> ,  2 S.C.C.466.

RatioOfTheDecision	It is obvious that if a case could be transferred un der section 406 of the Code from a Special Judge it could only be transferred to another Special Judge or a court of superior jurisdiction but subordinate to <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	No such court exists.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, under this section the power of transfer can only be from one Special Judge to another Special Judge.
RatioOfTheDecision	Under section 407 however, corresponding to section 526 of the old Code, it was submitted <ORG> has power to transfer any case to itself for being tried by it, it was submitted.
Precedent	It appears to us that in <CITATION_351344> ,  2 S.C.R.
Precedent	678 an identical question arose.
Precedent	The petitioner in that case was a member of <ORG> serving in the State of Rajasthan.
Precedent	The State Government ordered his trial before the Special Judge of Bharatpur for offences under section 120B / 161 of the Indian Penal Code and under sections 5(1)(a) and (d) and 5(2) of the Act.

Precedent	Section 7(1) of the  Act required the offences involved in that case to be tried by a Special Judge only, and section 7(2) of the Act required the offences to be tried by a Special Judge for the area within which these were committed which condition could never be satisfied if there was a transfer.
Precedent	This Court held that the condition in sub-section (1) of section 7 of the Act that the case must be tried by a Special Judge, is a sine qua non for the trial of offences under section 6 .
Precedent	This condition can be satisfied by transferring the case from one Special Judge to another Special Judge.
Precedent	Sub-section (2) of section 7 merely distributes, it was noted, work between Special Judges appointed in a State with reference to territory.
Precedent	This provision is at par with the section of the Code which confers territorial jurisdiction on Sessions Judges and magistrates.
RatioOfTheDecision	An order of transfer by the very nature of things must sometimes result in taking the case out of the territory.
RatioOfTheDecision	The third sub-section of section 8 of the Act preserves the applica tion of any provision of the Code if it is not inconsistent with the Act save as provided by the first two sub-sections of that Section.
Argument	It was held by this <ORG> that section 527 of the old Code, hence, remains applicable if it is not inconsistent with section 7(2) of the Act.
Argument	It was held that there was no inconsistency between section 527 of the Code and  section 7(2) of the Act as the territorial jurisdiction created by the latter operates in a different sphere and under different circumstances.
Argument	Inconsistency can only be found if two provisions of law apply in identical circumstances, and create contradictions.
Argument	Such a situation does not arise when either this <ORG> or the High <ORG> exercises the power of transfer.
Argument	Therefore, this <ORG> in exercise of its jurisdiction and power under section 521 of the Code can transfer a case from a Special Judge subordinate to one High <ORG> to another Special Judge subordinate to another High <ORG>.
Argument	It has to be emphasised that that decision was confined to the power un der section 527 of the previous Code and to transfer from one Special to another Special Judge though of another State.
Argument	It was urged by <NAME> that <NAME>'s case (supra) being one of transfer from one Special Judge to another the judgment is not an authority for the proposition that it cannot be transferred to a court other than that of a Special Judge or to <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	But whatever be the position, this is no longer open at this juncture.
RatioOfTheDecision	The jurisdiction, it was submitted, created by section 7 of the Act of 1952 is of exclusiveness qua the <ORG> subordinate to <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is not exclusive qua a <ORG> of superior jurisdiction including a <ORG> which can hear an appeal against its decision.
RatioOfTheDecision	The non obstante clause does not prevail over other provisions of the Code such as those which recognise the powers of the superior courts to exercise jurisdiction on transfer.
Argument	It was submitted that the power of transfer vested in <ORG> is exercisable qua Special Judges and is recognised not  merely by <NAME>'s case but in earlier cases also, <NAME> submitted.
Argument	It was next submitted that apart from the power under sections 406 and 407 of the Code the power of transfer is also exercisable by <ORG> under Article 228 of the Constitution.
Argument	There' is no doubt that under this Article the case can be withdrawn from <ORG>.
Argument	It is open to <ORG> to finally dispose it of.
Argument	A chartered <ORG> can make orders of transfer under clause 29 of the Letters Patent.
Argument	Article 134(1)(b) of the Constitution expressly recognises the existence of such power in every <ORG>.
Argument	It was further submitted that any case transferred for trial to <ORG> in which it exercises jurisdiction only by reason of the order of transfer is a case tried not in ordinary original criminal jurisdiction but in extraordinary original criminal jurisdiction.
RulingByLowerCourt	Some <ORG>s had both ordinary criminal jurisdiction as well as extraordinary criminal original jurisdiction.
RulingByLowerCourt	The former was po ssessed by <ORG> Bombay, Madras and Calcutta.
RatioOfTheDecision	The first two <ORG> abolished it in the 40's and <ORG> continued it for quite some time and after the 50's in a truncated form until it was finally done away with by the Code .
RatioOfTheDecision	After the Code the only original criminal jurisdiction possessed by all the <ORG> is extraordinary.
RatioOfTheDecision	It can arise by transfer under the Code or the Constitution or under clause 29 of the Letters Patent.
Fact	It was submitted that it was not right that extraordinary original criminal jurisdiction is contained only in clause 24 of the Letters Patent of the Bombay High Court.
Fact	This is contrary to section 374 of the Code itself.
Fact	That refers to all <ORG> and not merely all or any one of the three Chartered <ORG>.
Fact	<CITATION_1239298> ,  Criminal Law Journal 545, <ORG> recognised its extraordinary original criminal jurisdiction as the only one that it possessed.
Precedent	The nature of this jurisdiction is clearly explained in Madura, Tirupparank undram etc.
Precedent	v. <NAME> and <NAME>, 35 Calcutta Weekly Notes, 1088 and <CITATION_546089> , A.I.R.
Precedent	1954 Calcutta 305, paragraph 15.
Fact	Reference may also be made to the Law Commissioner's 41st Report, paragraphs 3.1 to 3.6 at page 29 and paragraph 31.
Fact	10 at page 259.
Fact	The 1952 Act was passed to provide for speedier trial but the procedure evolved should not be so directed, it was submitted, that it would violate Article 14 as was held in <NAME> case (supra).
Fact	Section 7 of the 1952 Act provides that notwithstanding anything contained in the Code of Criminal Procedure, or in any other law the offences specified in sub-section (1) of section 6 shall be triable by Special Judges only.
Fact	So the law provides for a trial by Special Judge only and this is notwithstanding anything contained in sections 406 and 407 of the Code of Criminal Procedure, 1973.
Fact	Could it, therefore, be accepted that this Court exercised a power not given to it by <ORG> or the Constitution and acted under a power not exercisable  by it?
Fact	The question that has to be asked and answered is if a case is tried by a Special Judge or a court subordinate to <ORG> against whose order an appeal or a revision would lie-to <ORG>, is transferred by this <ORG> to <ORG> and such right of appeal or revision is taken away would not an accused be in a worse position than others?
RatioOfTheDecision	This <ORG> in <CITATION_1586918> ,  2 S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	495 did not refer either to section 406 or section 407 of the Code.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is only made dear that if the application had been made to <ORG> under section 407 of the Code, <ORG> might have transferred the case to itself The second question that arises here is if such a wrong direction has been given by this <ORG> can such a direction inter-parties be challenged subsequently.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is really a value perspective judgement.
Precedent	<CITATION_1625415> , l 19551 1 S.C.R.
Precedent	117 at 121 <NAME>, J.
Argument	Observed that the fundamental principle is well established that a decree passed by a <ORG> without juris diction is a nullity, and that its validity could be set up whenever and wherever it is sought to be enforced or relied upon-even at the stage of execution and even in collateral proceedings.
RatioOfTheDecision	A defect of jurisdiction whether it is pecuniary or territorial, or whether it is in respect of the subject-matter of the action, strikes at the very authority of the <ORG> to pass any decree, and such a defect cannot be cured even by consent of parties.
Precedent	This question has been well put, if we may say so, in the decision of this <ORG> in <CITATION_340501> ,  1 S.C.R.
Precedent	697 where <NAME>, J.
Argument	Observed that the jurisdiction was a verbal <NAME> of many colours and referred to the decision in <CITATION_1252118> ,  2 A.C. 147 where the majority of <ORG> dealt with the assimilation of the concepts of 'lack' and 'excess' of jurisdiction or, in other words, the extent to which we have moved away from the traditional concept of jurisdiction.
Argument	The effect of the dicta was to reduce the difference between jurisdictional error  and error of law within jurisdiction almost to a vanishing point.
Argument	What is a wrong decision on a question of limitation, he posed referring to an article of Professor <NAME>, "Constitutional and Administrative Aspects of the Anismanic case" and concluded; "it is a bit difficult to understand how an erroneous decision on a question of limitation or res judicata would oust the jurisdiction of the <ORG> in the primitive sense of the term and render the decision or decree embodying the decision a nullity liable to collateral attack .. And there is no yardstick to determine the magnitude of the error other than the opinion of the <ORG>."
RatioOfTheDecision	(Emphasis supplied) While applying the ratio to the facts of the present controversy, it has to be borne in mind that section 7(1) of the 1952 Act creates a condition which is sine qua non for the trial of offenders under section 6(1) of that Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this connection, the offences specified under section 6(1) of the 1952 Act are those punishable under sections 161 , 162 , 50  1 63 , 164 and 165A of the Indian Penal Code and section 5 of the 1947 Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, the order of this <ORG> transferring the cases to <ORG> on 16th February, 1984, was not authorised by law.
Precedent	This <ORG>, by its directions could not confer jurisdiction on <ORG> of Bombay to try any case which it did not possess such jurisdiction under the scheme of the 1952 Act.
Precedent	It is true that in the first judgment in <CITATION_1502681> ,  2 S.C.R.
Precedent	914 when this <ORG> was analysing the scheme of the 1952 Act, it referred to sections 6 and 7 at page 931 of the Reports.
Precedent	The arguments, however, were not advanced and it does not appear that this aspect with its remifications was present in the mind of the <ORG> while giving the impugned directions.
Precedent	<NAME> sought to urge before us that the order made by the <ORG> was not without jurisdiction or irregular.
RatioOfTheDecision	We are unable to agree.
RatioOfTheDecision	It appears to us that the order was quite clearly per incuriam.
RatioOfTheDecision	This <ORG> was not called upon and did not decide the express  limitation on the power conferred by section 407 of the Code which includes offences by public servants mentioned in the 1952 Act to be overridden in the manner sought to be followed as the consequential direction of this <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	This <ORG>, to be plain, did not have jurisdiction to transfer the case to itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	That will be evident from an analysis of the different provisions of the Code as well as the 1952 Act.
Precedent	The power to create or enlarge jurisdiction is legislative in character, so also the power to confer a right of appeal or to take away a right of appeal.
Precedent	<ORG> alone can do it by law and no <ORG>.
Precedent	whether superior or inferior or both combined can enlarge the jurisdiction of a <ORG> or divest a person of his rights of revision and appeal.
Precedent	See in this connection the observations in <CITATION_340501> (supra) in which Justice <NAME> considered <NAME>,  2 AC 147 and also see Halsbury's Laws of England, 4th Edn.
Precedent	Vol.
Precedent	10 page 327 at para 720 onwards and also <NAME>Jurisdiction and Illegali ty' (1965 Edn.
Precedent	pages 16-50).
Precedent	Reference may also be made to <CITATION_539740> ,  2 SCR 800.
Precedent	The question of validity, however, is important in that the want of jurisdiction can be established solely by a superior <ORG> and that, in practice, no decision can be impeached collaterally by any inferior <ORG>.
Precedent	But the superior <ORG> can always correct its own error brought to its notice either by way of petition or ex debito justitiae.
Precedent	See Rubinstein's Jurisdiction and Illegality' (supra).
Argument	In the aforesaid view of the matter and the principle reiterated, it is manifest that the appellant has not been ordered to be tried by a procedure mandated by law, but by a procedure which was violative of Article 21 of the Constitution.
Argument	That is violative of Articles 14 and 19 of the Constitution also, as is evident from the observations of the <ORG> judgment in <NAME> case (supra) where this <ORG> found that even for a criminal who was alleged to have committed an offence, a special trial would be per se  illegal because it will deprive the accused of his substantial and valuable privileges of defences which, others similarly charged, were able to claim.
Argument	As Justice <NAME> observed in the said decision at page 366 of the report, it matters not whether it was done in good faith, whether it was done for the convenience of Government, whether the process could be scientifically classified and labelled, or whether it was an experiment for speedier trial made for the good of society at large.
Argument	Justice <NAME> emphasised that it matters not how lofty and laudable the motives were.
RatioOfTheDecision	The question which must be examined is, can fair minded, reasonable, unbiased and resolute men regard that with equanimity and call it reasonable, just and fair, regard it as equal treatment and protection in the defence of liberties which is expected of a sovereign democratic republic in the conditions which are obtained in India today.
RulingByPresentCourt	Judged by that view the singling out of the appellant in this case for a speedier trial by <ORG> rt for an offence of which <ORG> had no jurisdiction to try under the Act of 1952 was, in our opinion, unwarranted, unprecedented and the directions given by this <ORG> for the said purpose, were not warranted.

Argument	The question was whether Rule 12 of order XXXV of the Supreme <ORG> Rules empowered the Supreme <ORG> in writ petitions under Article 32 to require the petitioner to furnish security for the costs of the respondent.
Argument	Article 145 of the Constitution provides for the rules to be made subject to any law made by <ORG> and Rule 12 was framed thereunder.
Argument	The petitioner contended that the rule was invalid as it placed obstructions on the fundamental right guaranteed under Article 32 to move the Supreme <ORG> for the enforcement  of fundamental rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	This rule as well as the judicial order dismissing the petition under Article 32 of the Constitution for non-compliance with Rule 12 of order XXXV of the Supreme Court Rules were held invalid.
RatioOfTheDecision	In order to appreciate the significance of this point and the actual ratio of that decision so far as it is relevant for our present purpose it is necessary to refer to a few facts of that decision.
Fact	The petitioner and 8 others who were partners of <ORG>, Ghaziabad, had filed under Article 32 of the Constitution a petition impeaching the validity of the order passed by the Excise Commissioner refusing permission to the <ORG> to supply power alcohol to the said petitioners.
Fact	The petition was admitted on 12th December, 1961 and a rule was ordered to be issued to the respondents, the Excise Commissioner of U.P., Allahabad, and the State of U.P.
Fact	At the time when the rule was issued, this Court directed under the impugned rule that the petitioners should deposit a secu rity Of Rs.2,500 in cash within six weeks.
Fact	According to the practice of <ORG> prevailing since 1959, this order was treated as a condition precedent for issuing rule nisi to the impleaded respondents.
Fact	The petitioners found it difficult to raise the amount and so on January 24, 1962, they moved this <ORG> for modification of the said order as to security.
Fact	This application was dismissed, but the petitioners were given further time to deposit the said amount by March 26, 1962.
Fact	This order was passed on March 15, 1962.
Fact	The petioners then tried to collect the requisite fund, but failed in their efforts and that led to the said petition filed on March 24, 1962 by the said petitioners.
Fact	The petitioners contended that the impugned rule, in so far as it related to the giving of security, was ultra vires, because it contravened the fundamental right guaranteed to the petitioners under Article 32 of the Constitution.
Fact	There were two orders, namely, one for security of costs and another for the dismissal of the previ ous application under Article 32 of the Constitution.
Fact	This <ORG> by majority held that Rule 12 of order XXXV of the Supreme <ORG> Rules was invalid in so far as it related to the furnishing of security.
Argument	The right to move the Supreme <ORG>, it was emphasised, under Article 32 was an absolute right and the content of this right could not be circumscribed or impaired on any ground and an order for furnishing security for the respondent's costs retarded the assertion or vindication of the fundamental right under Article 32 and contravened the said right.
Argument	The fact that the rule was discretionary did not alter the position.
Argument	Though Article 142(1) empowers the Supreme <ORG> to pass any order to do complete justice between the parties, the <ORG> cannot make an order inconsistent with the fundamental rights guaranteed by Part III of the Constitution.
Argument	No question of inconsistency between Article 142(1) and Article 32 arose.
Argument	<NAME>, J. speaking for the majority of the Judges of this <ORG> said that Article F 142 (1) did not confer any power on this <ORG> to contravene The provisions of Article 32 of the Constitution.
Argument	Nor did Article 145 confer power upon this <ORG> to make rules, empowering it to contravene the provisions of the fundamental right.
Argument	At page 899 of the Reports, <NAME>, J. reiterated that the powers of this <ORG> are no doubt very wide and they are intended and "will always be exercised in the interests of justice."
Argument	But that is not to say that an order can be made by this <ORG> which is inconsistent with the fundamental rights guaranteed by Part III of the Constitution.
Argument	It was emphasised that an order which this <ORG> could make in order to do complete justice between the parties, must not only be consistent with the fundamental rights guaranteed by the Constitution, but it cannot even be inconsistent with the substantive provisions of the relevant statutory laws (Emphasis A supplied).
Argument	The <ORG> therefore, held that it was not possible to hold that Article 142(1) conferred upon this <ORG> powers  which could contravene the provisions of Article 32.

Argument	Furthermore, it violates Article 14 of the Constitution as being made applicable to a very special case among The special cases, without any guideline as to which cases required speedier justice.
Argument	If that was so as in <NAME> case, that was a mistake of so great a magnitude that it deprives a man by being treated differently of his fundamental right for defending himself in a criminal tr ial in accordance with law.
RatioOfTheDecision	If that was so then when the attention of the <ORG> is drawn the <ORG> has always the power and the obligation to correct it ex debito justitiae and treat the second application by its inherent power as a power of review to correct the original mistake.
RatioOfTheDecision	No suitor should suffer for the wrong of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	This <ORG> in <NAME> case struck down not only the administrative order enjoined by Rule 12 for deposit of security in a petition under Article 32 of the Constitution but also struck down the judicial order passed by the <ORG> for non- deposit of such security in the subsequent stage of the same proceeding when attention of the <ORG> to the infirmity of the rule was drawn.

Argument	<NAME> further submitted that we should not only ignore the directions or set aside the directions contained in the order dated 16th February, 1984, but also direct that the appellant should not suffer any further trial.

RatioOfTheDecision	We are, however, not inclined to go into this question.
RatioOfTheDecision	The right of appeal under section 374 is limited to Clause 24 of Letters Patent.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was further submitted that the expression 'Extraordinary original criminal jurisdiction' under section 374 has to be understood having regard to the language used in the Code and other relevant statutory provisions and not with reference to decisions wherein Courts described jurisdiction acquired by transfer as extraordinary original jurisdiction.

RatioOfTheDecision	It appears to us that there is good deal of force in the argument that- section 411A of the old Code which corresponds to section 374 of the new Code contained the expression 'original jurisdiction'.
RatioOfTheDecision	The new Code abolished the origina l jurisdiction of <ORG> but retained the extraordinary original criminal jurisdiction conferred by clause 24 of the Letters Patent which some of the <ORG> had.
RatioOfTheDecision	The right of appeal is, therefore, confined only to cases decided by <ORG> in its Letter Patent jurisdiction which in terms is `extraordinary original criminal jurisdiction'.
RatioOfTheDecision	By the time the new Code of Criminal Procedure 1973 was framed, Article 21 had not been interpreted so as to include one right of appeal both on facts and law.
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME> made elaborate submissions before us regarding the purpose of the Criminal Law Amendment Act and the constitution of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	In our opinion, these submissions have no relevance and do not authorise this Court to confer a special jurisdiction on <ORG> not warranted by the statute.

RatioOfTheDecision	Similarly, the observations on right of appeal in <CITATION_220229> ,  3  SCR 500, <NAME> brought to our notice certain facts to say that the powers given in the Criminal Law Amendment Act were sought to be misused by <ORG> under the influence of the appellant.
RatioOfTheDecision	In our opinion, these submissions are not relevant for the present purpose.
RatioOfTheDecision	Mr. <NAME> submitted that the argument that in so far as section 407 purports to authorise such a transfer it stands repealed by section 7(1) of the Criminal Law Amendment Act is wrong.
RatioOfTheDecision	He said it can be done in its extraordinary criminal jurisdiction.
RatioOfTheDecision	We are unable to accept this submission.
RatioOfTheDecision	We are also unable to accept the submission that the order of transfer was made with full knowledge of section 7(1) of the Criminal Law Amendment Act and the so- called exclusive jurisdiction was taken away from Special Judges and the directions were not given per incuriam.
RatioOfTheDecision	That is not right.
RatioOfTheDecision	He drew our attention to the principles of interpretation of statutes and drew our attention to the purpose of section 7(1) of the Act.
Argument	He subm itted that when the Amending Act changes the law, the change must be confined to the mischief present and intended to be dealt with.
Argument	He drew us to the <ORG> and submitted that he did not wish that an occasional case withdrawn and tried in <ORG> was because of delay in disposal of corruption cases.
Argument	He further submitted that interference with existing jurisdiction and powers of superior Courts can only be by express and clear language.
Argument	It cannot be brought about by a side wind.
Argument	Thirdly, the Act of 1952 and the Code have to be read and construed together, he urged.
Argument	The Court is never anxious to discover a repugnancy and infer apro tanto repeal.
Argument	Resort to the non obstante clause is permissible only when it is impossible to harmonise the two provisions.
Argument	<NAME> highlighted before us that it was for the first time a Chief Minister had been found guilty of receiving quid pro quo for orders of allotment of cement to various builders by a Single Judge of <ORG> confirmed b y a Division Bench of <ORG>.
Argument	He also urged before us that it was for the first time such a Chief Minister did not have the courage to prosecute his special leave petition before this <ORG> against the findings of three Judges of <ORG>.
Argument	<NAME> also urged that it was for the first time this <ORG> found that a case instituted in 1982 made no progress till 1984.
Argument	<NAME> also sought to contend that section 7(1) of the 1952 Act states "shall be triable by Special Judges only", but does not say that under no circumstances the case will be transferred to be tried by <ORG> even in its Extraordinary original Criminal Jurisdiction.
Argument	He submitted that section 407(1)(iv) is very much in the statute and and it is not repealed in respect of the cases pending before the Special Judge.
Argument	There is no question of repealing section 407(1)(iv) .
Argument	Section 407 deals with the power of <ORG> to transfer cases and appeals.
Argument	Section 7 is entirely different and one has to understand the sc heme of the Act of 1952, he urged.
RulingByLowerCourt	It was an Act which provided for a more speedy trial of certain offences.
RulingByLowerCourt	For this it gave power to appoint Special Judges and stipulated for appointment of Special Judges under the Act.
RulingByLowerCourt	Section 7 states that notwithstanding anything contained in the Code , the offences mentioned in sub-section (1) of section 6 shall be triable by Special Judges only.
RulingByLowerCourt	By express terms therefore, it takes away the right to transfer cases contained in the Code to any other <ORG> which is not a Special <ORG>.
RulingByLowerCourt	<NAME> sought to urge that <ORG> had considered this position.
RulingByLowerCourt	That is not so.
RulingByLowerCourt	He submitted that the directions of this <ORG> on 16th February, 1984 were not given per incuriam or void for any reason.
Precedent	He referred us to <NAME> on jurisprudence, 5th Edition, page 128 and relied on the decision of <NAME> v. <ORG>, [1975] 3 All E.R.
Precedent	801 at 821.
Precedent	He submitted that the per incuriam rule A does not apply where the previous authority is alluded to.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is true that previous statute is referred to in the other judgment delivered on the same date in connection with different contentions.
RatioOfTheDecision	Section 7(1) was not referred to in respect of the directions given on 16th February, 1984 in the case of <CITATION_1586918> (supra).
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, as mentioned hereinbefore the observations indubitably were per incuriam.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this case in view of the specific language used in section 7 , it is not necessary to consider the other submissions of <NAME>, whether the procedure for trial by Special Judges under the Code has stood repealed or not.
RatioOfTheDecision	The concept of repeal may have no application in this case.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is clear that words should normally be given their ordinary meaning bearing in mind the context.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is only where the literal meaning is not clear that one resorts to the golden rule of interpretation or the mischief rule of interpretation.
Precedent	This is well illustrated from the observations of <NAME>, C.J.
Precedent	in Sussex Peerage Claim, [18441 11 Cl & Fin 85 at 143.
Precedent	He observed:   "The only rule for the construction of Acts of <ORG> is that they should be construed according to the intent of the <ORG> which passed the Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the words of the statute are in themselves precise and unambiguous, then no more can be necessary than to expound those words in that natural and ordinary sense.
RatioOfTheDecision	The words themselves alone do, in such case, best declare the intention of the lawgiver.
RatioOfTheDecision	But if any doubt arises from the terms employed by the legislature, it has always been held a safe means of collecting the intention, to call in aid the ground and cause of making the statute, and to have recourse to the preamble, which, according to Chief Justice <NAME>, <NAME> v. Lord <NAME>, [1569] 1 Plowd 353 at 369 is a key to open the minds of the makers of the Act, and the mischiefs which they intend to redress".
RatioOfTheDecision	This passage states the commonly accepted view concerning the relationship between the literal and mischief rules of interpretation of statutes.
RulingByPresentCourt	Here there is no question as to what was the  previous law and what was intended to be placed or replaced as observed by Lord <NAME> in 274 House of Lords Debate, Col. 1294 on 16th November, 1966, see <ORG>, second edition, page 36.
RatioOfTheDecision	He observed that the interpretation of legislation is just a part of the process of being a good lawyer; a multi-faceted thing, calling for many varied talents; not a subject which can be confined in rules.
RatioOfTheDecision	67  When the words are clear nothing remains to be seen.
RatioOfTheDecision	If words are as such ambiguous or doubtful other aids come in.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this context, the submission of controversy was whether the Code repealed the Act of 1952 or whether it was repugnant or not is futile exercise to undertake.
Fact	<NAME> distinguished the decision in <NAME>'s case, which has already been discussed.
Fact	It is not necessary to discuss the controversy whether <ORG> contained the Extraordinary original Criminal Jurisdiction by the Letters Patent.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 134(1)(b) does not recognise in every <ORG>  power to withdraw for trial cases from any <ORG> subordinate to its authority.
RatioOfTheDecision	At least this Article cannot be construed to mean where power to withdraw is restricted, it can be widened by virtue of Article 134(1)(b) of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Section 374 of the Code undoubtedly gives a right of appeal.
RatioOfTheDecision	Where by a specific clause of a specific statute the power is given for trial by the Special Judge only and transfer can be from one such Judge to another Special Judge, there is no warrant to suggest that the High <ORG> has power to transfer such a case from a Judge under section 6 of the Act of 1952 to itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is not a case of exclusion of the superior <ORG>s.
RatioOfTheDecision	So the submissions made on this aspect by <NAME> are not relevant.
RatioOfTheDecision	Dealing with the submission that the order of <ORG> was void or non-est and it violated the principles of natural justice, it was submitted by <NAME> that it was factually incorrect.
RatioOfTheDecision	Inspite of the submissions the appellant did not make any submission as  to directions for transfer as asked for by <NAME>.

RatioOfTheDecision	The Court merely observed there that they had given ample direction.
RatioOfTheDecision	No question of submission arose after the judgment was delivered.
RatioOfTheDecision	In any case, if this was bad the fact that no objection had been raised would not make it good.
RatioOfTheDecision	No question of technical rules or res judicata apply, <NAME> submitted that it would amount to an abuse of the process of the Court.
Precedent	He referred us to Re Tarling, [1979] 1 All E.R.
Precedent	981 at 987; <CITATION_1204411> for <ORG>,  1 All E.R.
Precedent	1009 at 1014 and Seervai's Constitutional Law, Vol.
Precedent	1, pages 260 to 265.

Precedent	There is no abuse of the process of the Court.

Precedent	There was prejudice to the accused in being singled out as a special class of accused for a special dispensation without room for any appeal as of righ t and without power of the revision to <ORG>.
Precedent	There .
Precedent	prejudice in that.

Precedent	924 at 927 was not proper in the facts of this case.
Precedent	If a discrimination is brought about by judicial perception and not by executive whim, if it is unauthorised by law, it will be in derogation of the right of the appellant as the special procedure in <NAME> case (supra) curtailed the rights and privileges of the accused.
Precedent	Similarly, in this case by judicial direction the rights and privileges of the accused have been curtailed without any justification in law.
Precedent	Reliance was placed on the observations of the seven Judges Bench in Re: Special <ORG>s Bill, 1978 (supra).
Precedent	<NAME> relied on the said observations therein and emphasised that purity in public life is a desired goal at all times and in all situations and ordinary Criminal <ORG>s due to congestion of work cannot reasonably be expected to bring the prosecutions to speedy te rmination.
Argument	He further submitted that it is imperative that persons holding high public or political office must be speedily tried in the interests of justice.

RatioOfTheDecision	All this is true but the trial even of person holding public office though to be made speedily must be done in accordance with the procedure established by law.
RatioOfTheDecision	The provisions of section 6 read with section 7 of the Act of 1952 in the facts and circumstances of this case is the procedure established by law; any deviation even by a judicial direction will be negation of the rule of law.
RatioOfTheDecision	Our attention was drawn to Article 145(e) and it was submitted that review can be made only where power is expressly conferred and the review is subject to the rules made under Article 145(e) by <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The principle of finality on which the Article proceeds applies to both judgments an d orders made by <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	But directions given per incuriam and in violation of certain constitutional limitations and in derogation of the principles of natural justice can always be remedied by the court ex debite justitiae.
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME> submission was that ex debite justitiae, these directions could not be recalled.
RatioOfTheDecision	We are unable to agree with this submission.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> in <NAME> v. <NAME>, [1984] 3 A.E.R.
RulingByLowerCourt	140 held that orders made by a <ORG> of unlimited jurisdiction in the course of contentious litigation are either regular or irregular.
RulingByLowerCourt	If an order is regular it can only be set aside by an appellate <ORG>; if it is irregular it can be set aside by the <ORG> that made it on application being made to that <ORG> either under rules of <ORG> dealing expressly with setting aside orders for irregularity or ex debite justitiae if the circumstances warranted, namely, where there was a breach of the rules of natural justice etc.
RulingByLowerCourt	<NAME> urged before us that Lord <NAME> had in e xpress terms rejected the argument that any orders of a superior <ORG> of unlimited jurisdiction can over be void in the sense that they can be ignored with impunity.
RulingByLowerCourt	We are not concerned with that.
RulingByLowerCourt	Lord <NAME> delivered the judgment.
Precedent	Another Judge who sat in <ORG> with him was Lord <NAME> of Kinkel.
Precedent	Both these Law Lords were parties to the <ORG> judgment in Re <ORG> .
Precedent	case [1980] 2 A.E.R.
Precedent	634 and their Lordships did not extend this principle any further.

Precedent	Lord <NAME> refused to extend the Anisminic principle to superior <ORG>s by the felicitous statement that this amounted to comparison of incomparables.
Precedent	We are not concerned with this controversy.
Precedent	We are not comparing incomparables.
Precedent	We are correcting an irregularity committed by <ORG> not on construction or misconstruction of a statute but on non-perception of certain provisions and certain authorities which wo uld amount to derogation of the constitutional rights of the citizen.
Precedent	The directions given by the order of 16th February, 1984 at page 557 were certainly without hearing though in the presence of the parties.
Precedent	Again consequential upon directions these were challenged ultimately in this Court and finally this Court reserved the right to challenge these by an appropriate application.
RatioOfTheDecision	The directions were in deprival of Constitutional rights and contrary to the express provisions of the Act of 1952.
RatioOfTheDecision	The directions were given in violation of the principles of natural justice.
RatioOfTheDecision	The directions were without precedent in the background of the Act of 1952.
RatioOfTheDecision	The directions definitely deprived the appellant of certain rights of appeal and revision and his rights under the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	We do not labour ourselves on the question of discretion to disobey a judicial order on the ground of invalid judicial order.
RatioOfTheDecision	See discretion to Disobey by <NAME> and <NAME> pages 111 and 112.
RatioOfTheDecision	These directions were vo id because the power was not there for this <ORG> to transfer a proceeding under the Act of 1952 from one Special Judge to the High <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is not a case of collateral attack on judicial proceeding; it is a case where the <ORG> having no <ORG> superior to it rectifies its own order.
RatioOfTheDecision	We recognise that the distinction between an error which entails absence of jurisdiction and an error made within the jurisdiction is very fine.
Precedent	So fine indeed that it is rapidly being eroded as observed by Lord <NAME> in <ORG> v. Foreign Compensation Commissioner, [1959] 1 All E.R.
Precedent	208 at 244.
Precedent	Having regard to the enormity of the consequences of the error to the appellant and by reason of the fact that the directions were given suo motu, we do not find there is anything in the observations of <CITATION_861817> , [19641 1 S.C.R.
Precedent	495 which detract the power of the <ORG> to review its judgment ex debite justitiae in case injustice has been caused.
RatioOfTheDecision	No court, however, high has jurisdiction to give an order unwarrante d by the Constitution and, therefore, the principles of <CITATION_732701> ,  S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	185 at 190 would not apply.
RatioOfTheDecision	ln giving the directions this <ORG> infringed the Constitutional safeguards granted to a citizen or to an accused and injustice results therefrom.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is just and proper for the <ORG> to rectify and recall that in justice, in the peculiar facts and circumstances of this case This case has caused us considerable anxiety.
RatioOfTheDecision	The appellant accused has held an important position in this country, being the Chief Minister of a premier State of the country.
Fact	He has been charged with serious criminal offences.
RatioOfTheDecision	His trial in accordance with law and the procedure established by law would have to be in accordance with the 1952 Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	That could not possibly be done because of the directions of this <ORG> dated 16th February, 1984, as indicated above.
RatioOfTheDecision	It has not yet been found whether the appellant is guilty or innocent.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is unfortunate, unfortunate for the people of the State, unfortunate for the country as a whole , unfortunate for the future working of democracy in this country which, though is not a plant of an easy growth yet is with deep root in the Indian polity that delay has occurred due to procedural wrangles.
RatioOfTheDecision	The appellant may be guilty of grave offences alleged against him or he may be completely or if not completely to a large extent, innocent.
RatioOfTheDecision	Values in public life and perspective of these values in public life, have undergone serious changes and erosion during the last few decades.
RatioOfTheDecision	What was unheard of before is common place today.
RatioOfTheDecision	A new value orientation is being undergone in our life and in our culture.
RatioOfTheDecision	We are at the threshold of the cross-roads of values.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is, for the sovereign people of the country to settle those conflicts yet the <ORG> have vital roles to play in such matters.
RatioOfTheDecision	With the avowed object of speedier trial the case of the appellant had been transferred to <ORG> but on grounds of expediency of trial he cannot be subjected to a procedure unwarranted by law, and contrary to the con stitutional provisions.
RatioOfTheDecision	The appellant may or may not be an ideal politician.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is a fact, however, that the allegations have been brought against him by a person belonging to a political party opposed to his but that is not the decisive factor.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the appellant <NAME> has infringed law, he must be dealt with in accordance with the law.
RatioOfTheDecision	We proclaim and pronounce that no man is above the law, but at the same time reiterate and declare that no man can be denied his rights under the Constitution and the laws.
RatioOfTheDecision	He has a right to be dealt with in accordance with the law and not in derogation of it.
RatioOfTheDecision	This Court?
RatioOfTheDecision	in its anxiety to facilitate the parties to have a speedy trial gave directions on 16th February, 1984 as mentioned hereinbefore without conscious awareness of the exclusive jurisdiction of <ORG> under the 1952 Act and that being the only procedure established by law, there can be no deviation from the terms of Article 21 of the Constitution of India.
RatioOfTheDecision	That is the only procedure  under which it should have been guided.
RatioOfTheDecision	By reason of giving the directions on 16th February, 1984 this <ORG> had also unintentionally caused the appellant the denial of rights under Article 14 of the Constitution by denying him the equal protection of law by being singled out for a special procedure not provided for by law.
RatioOfTheDecision	When these factors are brought to the notice of this <ORG>, even if there are any technicalities this <ORG> should not feel shackled and decline to rectify that injustice or other vise the injustice noticed will remain forever a blot on justice.
RatioOfTheDecision	It has been said long time ago that "Actus Curiae Neminem Gravabit"-an act of the <ORG> shall prejudice no man.
RatioOfTheDecision	This maxim is founded upon justice and good sense and affords a safe and certain guide for the administration of the law.
Precedent	Lord <NAME> in <NAME> v. <ORG>, (Law Reports Vol.
Precedent	III 1869-71 page 465 at page 475) observed thus:  "Now, their Lordships are of opinion, that one of the first and highest duties  of all <ORG> is to take care that the act of the <ORG> does no injury to any of the Suitors, and when the expression 'the act of the <ORG>' is used, it does not mean merely the act of the Primary <ORG>, or of any intermediate <ORG> of appeal, but the act of the <ORG> as a whole, from the lowest <ORG> which entertains jurisdiction over the matter up to the highest <ORG> which finally disposes of the case.
Precedent	It is the duty of the aggregate of those <ORG>, if I may use the expression, to take care that no act of the <ORG> in the course of the whole of the proceedings does an injury to the suitors in the <ORG>."
Precedent	72  This passage was quoted in the Gujarat High <ORG> by <NAME> J. speaking for the Gujarat High <ORG> in <CITATION_351056> (supra) as mentioned before.
Precedent	It appears that in giving directions on 16th February, 1984, this <ORG> acted per incuriam inasmuch it did not bear in mind consciously the consequences and the provisions of sections 6 and 7 of the 1952 Act and the binding nature of the larger B ench decision in <NAME> case (supra) which was not adverted to by this <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The basic fundamentals of the administration of justice are simple.
RatioOfTheDecision	No man should suffer because of the mistake of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	No man should suffer a wrong by technical procedure of irregularities.
RatioOfTheDecision	Rules or procedures are the hand-maids of justice and not the mistress of the justice.
RatioOfTheDecision	Ex debite justitiae, we must do justice to him.
RatioOfTheDecision	If a man has been wronged so long as it lies within the human machinery of administration of justice that wrong must be remedied.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is a peculiar fact of this case which requires emphasis.
Argument	<NAME>, learned counsel for the appellant has vehemently canvassed before us that the appellant has suffered a great wrong for over six and a half years.
Argument	He has undergone trials and proceedings because of the mistakes of the <ORG>.
Argument	<NAME> submitted that the appellant should be made not to suffer more.
RatioOfTheDecision	Counsel urged that political battles must be fought in the political arena.
RatioOfTheDecision	Yet a charge of infraction  of law cannot remain uninvestigated against an erstwhile Chief Minister of a premier State of the country.
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME> has canvassed before us on the authority of <CITATION_1007347> ,  3 S.C.R.
Precedent	169 at 179-180; Kadra Pahadiyal (1) v. State of Bihar, A.I.R.
Precedent	1981 S.C. 939; <CITATION_770248> , A.I.R.
Precedent	1982 S.C. 1167 and <CITATION_595461> , A.I.R.
Precedent	1986 S.C. 1773.
Precedent	He has, however, very strongly relied upon the observations of this Court in <NAME> v. Union Territory of Arunachal Pradesh (supra).
Precedent	In that case the appellant a government servant was tried and convicted to suffer imprisonment for two years for offences under Section 506 read with Section 34 , I.P.C .
Precedent	He was not represented at the trial by any lawyer by reason of his inability to afford legal representation.
Precedent	On appeal <ORG> held that the trial was not vitiated since no application for legal aid was made by him.
Precedent	On appeal this Court quashed the conviction and considered the question whether the appellant would have to be tried in accordance with la w after providing legal assistance to him.
Precedent	This Court felt that in the interests of justice the appellant should be reinstated in service without back wages and accordingly directed that no trial should take place.
RulingByPresentCourt	<NAME> submitted that we should in the facts of this case in the interests of justice direct that the appellant should not be tried again.
RulingByPresentCourt	<NAME> submitted to let the appellant go only on this long delay and personal inconveniences suffered by the appellant, no more injury be caused to him.
RatioOfTheDecision	We have considered the submission.
RatioOfTheDecision	Yet we must remind ourselves that purity of public life is one of the cardinal principal which must be upheld as a matter of public policy.
RatioOfTheDecision	Allegations of legal infractions and criminal infractions must be investigated in accordance with law and procedure established under the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even if he has been wronged, if he is allowed to be left in doubt that would cause more serious damage to the appellant.
RulingByPresentCourt	Public confidence in public administration should not be eroded a ny further.
RulingByPresentCourt	One wrong cannot be remedied by another wrong.
RulingByPresentCourt	ln the aforesaid view of the matter and having regard to the facts and circumstances of the case, we are of the opinion that the legal wrong that has been caused to the appellant should be remedied.
RulingByPresentCourt	Let that wrong be therefore remedied.
RulingByPresentCourt	Let right be done and in doing so let no more further injury be caused to public purpose.
RulingByPresentCourt	ln the aforesaid view of the matter the appeal is allowed; all proceedings in this matter subsequent to the directions of this Court on 16th February, 1984 as indicated before are set aside and quashed.
RulingByPresentCourt	The trial shail proceed in accordance with law, that is to say under the Act of 1952 as mentioned hereinbefore.
Fact	<NAME>, J: I have had the advantage of perusing the judgment proposed by my learned Brother <NAME>, J.
Fact	While I agree with the conclusion proposed by my esteemed Brother, keeping the importance of the matter, particularly the consequences the decision may generate as also the fact that I was a party to the tw o-Judge Bench decision of this Court reported in 1986 (2) SCC 716 in view, I propose to express my opinion separately.
Fact	<NAME>, the appellant, was the Chief Minister of the State of Maharashtra from 1980 till January 20, 1982, when he resigned his office but continued to be a member of <ORG>.
Fact	<NAME>, Respondent No.
Fact	I herein, lodged a complaint in <ORG>, 28th Esplanade, Bombay, on September ll, 1981, against <NAME> alleging commission of several offences under the lndian Penal Code as also Section 5(2) of the Prevention of Corruption Act, 1947 ('1947 Act' for short).
Fact	The learned Magistrate was of the view that prosecution under Sections 161 and 165 of the Penal Code and  Section 5 of the 1947 Act required sanction as a condition precedent and in its absence the complaint was not maintainable.
Fact	The Governor of Bombay later accorded sanction and the Respondent no.
Fact	1 filed a fresh complaint, this time in <ORG>  the Special Judge of Bombay, alleging the commission of those offences which had formed the subject- matter of the complaint before the Magistrate.
