Fact	That is how this seven-Judge <ORG> h as come to be constituted to hear the appeal.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is the settled position in law that jurisdiction of courts comes solely from the law of the land and cannot be exercised otherwise.
RatioOfTheDecision	So far as the position in this country is concerned conferment of jurisdiction is possible either by the provisions of the Constitution or by specific laws enacted by the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	For instance, Article 129 confers all the powers of a court of record on <ORG> including the power to punish for contempt of itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	Articles 131, 132, 133, 134, 135, 137, 138 and 139 confer different jurisdictions on <ORG> while Articles 225, 226, 227, 228 and 230 deal with conferment of jurisdiction on <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Instances of conferment of jurisdiction by specific law are very common.
RatioOfTheDecision	The laws of procedure both criminal and civil confer jurisdiction on different courts.
RatioOfTheDecision	Special jurisdiction is conferred by special statute.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is thus clear that jurisdiction can be exercised only when provided for either in the Constitu tion or in the laws made by the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Jurisdiction is thus the authority or power of the court to deal with a matter and make an order carrying binding force in the facts.
Precedent	In support of judicial opinion for this view reference may be made to the permanent edition of 'Words and Phrases Vol.
RatioOfTheDecision	23A' at page 164.
RatioOfTheDecision	It would be appropriate to refer to two small passages occurring at pages 174 and 175 of the Volume.
RatioOfTheDecision	At page 174, referring to the decision in <ORG> v. <ORG> it has been stated:  "Jurisdiction is the authority to hear and determine, and in order that it may exist the following are essential: (1) A court created by law, organized and sitting; (2) authority given it by law to hear and determine causes of the kind in question; (3) power given it by law to render a judgment such as it assumes to render; (4) authority over the parties to the case if the judgment is to bind them personally as a judgment in personam, which is acquired over the plaintiff by his appearance and subm ission of the matter to the court, and is acquired over the defendant by his voluntary appearance, or by service of process on him; (5) authority over the thing adjudicated upon its being located within the court s territory, and by actually seizing it if liable to be carried away; (6) authority to decide the question involved, which is acquired by the question being submitted to it by the parties for decision."
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 139A of the Constitution authorises this <ORG> to transfer cases from <ORG> to itself or from one <ORG> to another and is, therefore, not relevant for our purpose.
Statute	Section 406 of the Code empowers this <ORG> to transfer cases and appeals by providing:  "(1) Whenever it is made to appear to the Supreme <ORG> that an order under this section is expedient for the ends of justice, it may direct that any particular case of appeal be transferred from one <ORG> to another <ORG> or from a Criminal <ORG> subordinate to one <ORG> to another Criminal <ORG> of equal or superio r jurisdiction subordinate to another High Court.
Statute	(2) <ORG> may act under this section only on the application of the Attorney- General of India or of a party interested, and every such application shall be made by motion, which shall, except when the applicant is the Attorney-General of India or the Advocate-General of the State, be supported by affidavit or affirmation.
Statute	(3)...................".
Statute	The offences alleged to have been committed by the accused here are either punishable under the Penal Code or under Act 2 of 1947, both Of which could have been tried in an appropriate court under the Criminal Procedure Code ; but <ORG> by the Criminal Law Amendment Act 46 of 1952 (1952 Act for short) amended both the Penal Code as also the Criminal Procedure Code with a view to providing for a more speedy trial of certain offences.
Statute	The relevant sections of the 1952 Act are sections 6 , 7 , 8 , 9 and 10 .
Statute	For convenience, they are extracted below:  "6.
Statute	Power to appoint special Judges (1) The State  Government may, by notification in the Official Gazette, appoint as many special Judges as may be necessary for such area or areas as may be specified in the notification to try the following offences, namely,  (a) an offence punishable under section 161 , section 162 , section 163 , section 164 , section 165 or section 165A of the Indian Penal Code  or section 5 of the Prevention of Corruption Act, 1947 ;  (b) any conspiracy to commit or any attempt to commit or any abetment of any of the offences specified in clause (a).
Statute	(2) A persorn shall not be qualified for appointment as a special Judge under this Act unless he is, or has been, a Sessions Judge or an Additional Sessions Judge or an assistant Sessions Judge under the Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898 ."
Statute	"7.
Statute	Class triable by Special Judges (1) Notwithstanding anything contained in the Code of Criminal Procedure?
Statute	1898  or in any other law the offences specified in sub-section (1) of section 6 shall be triable by Special Judges only;  (2) Every offence s pecified in sub-section  (l) of section 6 shall be tried by the Special Judge for the area within which it was committed.
Statute	Or where there are more Special Judges than one for such area.
Statute	by such one of them as may be specified in this behalf by <ORG>.
Statute	(3) When trying any case, a Special Judge may also try any offence other than an offence specified in section 6 A with which the accused may, under the Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898 , be charged at the same trial".
Statute	8.
Statute	Procedure and powers of Special Judges (  1) A Special Judge may take cognizance of offences without the accused being committed to him for trial, and in trying the accused persons, shall follow the procedure prescribed by the Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898 , for the trial of warrant cases by Magistrates.
Statute	(2) A special Judge, may, with a view to obtaining the evidence of any person supposed to have been directly or indirectly concerned in, or privy to, an offence, tender a pardon to such person on condition of his making a full  and true disclosure of the whole circumstances within his knowledge relating to the offence and to every other person concerned, whether as principal or abettor, in the commission thereof; and any pardon so tendered shall, for the purposes of sections 339 and 339-A of the Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898 , be deemed to have been tendered under section 338 of that Code.
Statute	(3) Save as provided in sub-section ( 1 ) or sub-section (2), the provisions of the Code of Criminal Procedure 1898 , shall, so far as they are not inconsistent with this Act, apply to the proceedings before a Special Judge; and for the purposes of the said provisions, <ORG> shall be deemed to be <ORG> trying cases without a jury or without the aid of assessors and the person conducting a prosecution before a Special Judge shall be deemed to be a public prosecutor.
Statute	(3-A) In particular, and without prejudice to the generality of the provisions contained in sub- section (3), the provisions of sections 350  and 549 of the Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898 , shall, so far as may be.
Statute	apply to the proceedings before a Special Judge, and for the purposes of the said provisions a special Judge shall be deemed to be a Magistrate.
Precedent	(4) A special Judge may pass upon any person convicted by him any sentence authorized by law for punish-  81  ment of the offence of which such person is convicted."
Precedent	"9.
Precedent	Appeal and revision-<ORG> may exercise, so far as they may be applicable, all the powers conferred by Chapters XXXI and XXXII of the Code of Criminal Procedure, I898 (1; of 1898) on <ORG> as if <ORG> were <ORG> trying cases without a jury within the local limits of the jurisdiction of <ORG>. '
Precedent	"10.
Precedent	Transfer of certain pending cases-All cases triable by a special Judge under section 7 which, immediately before the commencement of this Act, were pending before any Magistrate shall, on such commencement, be forwarded for trial to the special Judge having jurisdictio n over such cases."
Precedent	On the ratio of the seven-Judge Bench decision of this <ORG> in the <CITATION_184660633> , [ 1952] SCR 284 the vires of this Act are not open to challenge.
Precedent	The majority of the learned Judges in <NAME> case expressed the view that it was open to the <ORG> to set up a special forum for expedient trial of particular class of cases.
RatioOfTheDecision	Section 7 ( l) has clearly provided that offences specified in sub-section (1) of section 6 shall be triable by the Special Judge only and has taken away the power of the courts established under the Code of Criminal Procedure to try those offences.
RatioOfTheDecision	Section 10 of the Act required all pending cases on the date of commencement of the Act to stand transferred to the respective Special Judge.
RatioOfTheDecision	Unless there be challenge to the provision creating exclusive jurisdiction of the Special Judge, the procedural law in the Amending Act is binding on courts as also the parties and no court is entitled to make orders contrary to the law which are binding.
RatioOfTheDecision	As lon g as section 7 of the Amending Act of 1952 hold the field it was not open to any court including the apex Court to act contrary to section 7(1) of the Amending Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	The power to transfer a case conferred by the Constitution or by section 406 of the Code of Criminal Procedure does not specifically relate to the special Court.
RatioOfTheDecision	Section 406 of the Code could perhaps be applied on the principle that the Special Judge was a subordinate court for transferring a case from one special Judge to another special Judge.
RatioOfTheDecision	That would he so because such a transfer would not contravene the mandate of section 7 ( l ) of the Amending Act of 1952 .
RatioOfTheDecision	While that may be so, the provisions for transfer, already referred to.
RatioOfTheDecision	do not authorise H transfer of a case pending in the court of a special Judge first to <ORG> and then to <ORG> for trial.
Precedent	A four Judge Bench in <CITATION_539740> ,  2 SCR 800 was considering the jurisdiction of <ORG> to deal with a matter <NAME>, as he then was, spoke for the court t hus:  "But if the learned Judge, as reported in the summary of the judgment, was of the opinion that <ORG> is competent to assume to itself jurisdiction which it does not otherwise possess, merely because an 'extra-ordinary situation' has arisen, with respect to the learned Judge, we are unable to approve of that view.
Precedent	By 'jurisdiction' is meant the extent of the power which is conferred upon the court by its Constitution to try a proceeding; its exercise cannot be enlarged because what the learned Judge calls an extra ordinary situation 'requires' the <ORG> to exercise it".
RatioOfTheDecision	Brother <NAME> in his elaborate judgment has come to the conclusion that the question of transferring the case from the court of the special Judge to <ORG> was not in issue before the five- Judge Bench.
RatioOfTheDecision	Mr. <NAME> in course of the argument has almost accepted the position that this was not asked for on behalf of the complainant at the hearing of the matter before <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	From a reading of the judgm ent of <ORG> it appears that the transfer was a suo motu direction of the court.
RatioOfTheDecision	Since this particular aspect of the matter had not been argued and counsel did not have an opportunity of pointing out the legal bar against transfer, the learned Judges of this Court obviously did not take note of the special provisions in section 7(1) of the 1952 Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	I am inclined to agree with Mr. <NAME> for Antulay that if this position had been appropriately placed, the direction for transfer from the court of exclusive jurisdiction to <ORG> would not have been made by <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is appropriate to presume that this Court never' intends to act contrary to law.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is no doubt that after <ORG> of <NAME> and <NAME>, <ORG> dismissed the writ petition and the special leave petitions on 17th April, 1984, by indicating that the petitioner could file an appropriate review petition or any other application which he may be entitled in law to file.
RatioOfTheDecision	no further action was taken until char ges Were framed on the basis of evidence of 57 witnesses and a mass of documents.
RatioOfTheDecision	After a gap of more than three years.
RatioOfTheDecision	want of jurisdiction of <ORG> was sought to be reagitated before the two-Judge Bench in the present proceedings.
RatioOfTheDecision	During this intervening period of three years or so a lot of evidence was collected by examining the prosecution witnesses and exhibiting documents.
RatioOfTheDecision	A learned Judge of <ORG> devoted his full time to the case.
RatioOfTheDecision	Mr. <NAME> pointed out to us in course of his argument that the evidence that has already been collected is actually almost three-fourths of what the prosecution had to put in.
RatioOfTheDecision	Court's time has been consumed, evidence has been collected and parties have been put to huge expenses.
RatioOfTheDecision	To entertain the claim of the appellant that the transfer of the case from the Special Judge to <ORG> was without authority of law at this point of time would necessarily wipe out the evidence and set the clock back by about four years.
RatioOfTheDecision	It may be that some of the witnes ses may no longer be available when the de novo trial takes place.
RatioOfTheDecision	Apart from these features, according to Mr. <NAME> to say at this stage that the DIRECTION given by a five-Judge Bench is not binding and, therefore, not operative will shake the confidence of the litigant public in the judicial process and in the interest of the system it should not be done.
RatioOfTheDecision	Long arguments were advanced on either side in support of their respective stands-the appellant pleading that the direction for transfer of the proceedings from the Special Judge to <ORG> was a nullity and Mr. <NAME> contending that <ORG> had exercised its powers for expediting the trial and the action was not contrary to law.
RatioOfTheDecision	Brother <NAME> has dealt with these submissions at length and I do not find any necessity to dwell upon this aspect in full measure.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the ultimate analysis I am satisfied that this <ORG> did not possess the power to transfer the proceedings from the Special Judge to <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME> has raised ob jection at this stage before the matter has been concluded.
RatioOfTheDecision	In case after a full dressed trial, he is convicted, there can be no doubt that the wise men in law will raise on his behalf, inter alia, the same contention as has been advanced now by way of challenge to the conviction.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the accused is really guilty of the offences as alleged by the prosecution there can be no two opinions that he should be suitably punished and the social mechanism of punishing the guilty must come heavily upon him.
RatioOfTheDecision	No known loopholes should be permitted to creep in and subsist so as to give a handle to the accused to get out of the net by pleading legal infirmity when on facts the offences are made out.
RatioOfTheDecision	The importance of this consideration should not be overlooked in assessing the situation as to whether the direction of this <ORG> as contained in the five-Judge Bench decision should be permitted to be questioned at this stage or not.
RatioOfTheDecision	Mr. <NAME> for <ORG> argued at length and Brother <NAME> has noticed all those contentions t hat by the change of the forum of the trial the accused has been prejudiced.
RatioOfTheDecision	Undoubtedly, by this process he misses a forum of appeal because if the trial was handled by a Special Judge, the first appeal would lie to <ORG> and further appeal by special leave could come before this <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the matter is tried by <ORG> there would be only one forum of appeal being this <ORG>, whether as of right or by way of special leave.
RatioOfTheDecision	The appellant has also contended that the direction violates Article 14 of the Constitution because he alone has been singled out and picked up for being treated differently from similarly placed accused persons.
RatioOfTheDecision	Some of these aspects cannot be overlooked with ease.
RatioOfTheDecision	I must, however, indicate here that the argument based upon the extended meaning given to the contents of Article 21 of the Constitution, though attractive have not appealed to me.
RatioOfTheDecision	One of the well-known principles of law is that decision made by a competent court should be taken as final subject to further pro ceedings contemplated by the law of procedure.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the absence of any further proceeding, the direction of <ORG> of 16th of February, 1984 became final and it is the obligation of everyone to implement the direction of the apex <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such an order of this <ORG> should by all canons of judicial discipline be binding on this <ORG> as well and cannot be interfered with after attaining finality.
RatioOfTheDecision	Brother <NAME> has referred to several authorities in support of his conclusion that an order made without jurisdiction is not a valid one and can be ignored, overlooked or brushed aside depending upon the situation.
RatioOfTheDecision	I do not propose to delve into that aspect in my separate judgment.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is a well-settled position in law that an act of the court should not injure any of the suitors.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> in the well-known decision of <NAME> v. <ORG>, [1871] 3 P.C.
RatioOfTheDecision	465 observed:-  "One of the first and highest duties of all courts is to take care that the act of the cour t does no injury to any of the suitors, and when the expression act of the court is used, it does not mean merely the act of the primary court, or of any intermediate court of appeal, but the act of the court as a whole, from the lowest court which entertains jurisdiction over the matter upto the highest court which finally disposes of the case.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is the duty of the aggregate of those <ORG>, if I may use the expression, to take care that no act of the court in the course of the whole of the proceed ings does an injury to the suitors in courts."
RatioOfTheDecision	Brother <NAME> has also reffered to several other authorities which support this view.
RatioOfTheDecision	Once it is found that the order of transfer by this <ORG> dated 16th of February, 1984, was not within jurisdiction by the direction of the transfer of the proceedings made by this <ORG>, the appellant should not suffer.
RatioOfTheDecision	What remains to be decided is the procedure by which the direction of the 16th of February, 1984, could be recalled or altered.
RatioOfTheDecision	There can be no doubt that  certiorari shall not lie to quash a judicial order of this <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	That is so on account of the fact that the Benches of this <ORG> are not subordinate to larger Benches thereof and certiorari is, therefore, not admissible for quashing of the orders made on the judicial side of the court.
RatioOfTheDecision	Mr. <NAME> had relied upon the ratio in the case of <CITATION_1945293> ,  1 SCR 885.
Fact	Brother <NAME> has dealt with this case at considerable length.
Fact	This <ORG> was then dealing with an Article 32 petition which had been filed to challenge the vires of rule 12 of order 35 of this <ORG>'s Rules.
Fact	<NAME>, J., as the learned Judge then was, spoke for himself and three of his learned brethren including the learned Chief Justice.
Precedent	The facts of the case as appearing from the judgment show that there was a judicial order directing furnishing of security of Rs.2,500 towards the respondent's costs an(l the majority judgment directed "In the result, the petition is allowed and the order passed against the petitioners on December 12 , 1961, calling upon them to furnish security of Rs.2,500 is set aside."
Precedent	<NAME>, J. who wrote a separate judgment upheld the vires of the rule and directed dismissal of the petition.
Precedent	The fact that a judicial order was being made the subject matter of a petition under Article 32 of the Constitution was not noticed and whether such a proceeding was tenable was not considered.
Precedent	A nine-Judge Bench of this Court in <CITATION_1643138> .,  3 SCR 744 referred to the judgment in <NAME> case (supra).
Precedent	<NAME>, CJ., who delivered the leading and majority judgment stated at page 765 of the Reports:  "ln support of his argument that a judicial decision can be corrected by this Court in exercise of its writ jurisdiction under Article 32(2 ), Mr. <NAME> has relied upon another decision of this Court in <CITATION_1945293> (supra) .
RatioOfTheDecision	In that case, the petitioner had been required to furnish security for the costs of the respondent under rule 12 of order 35 of the Supreme Court Rules.
RatioOfTheDecision	By his petition filed unde r Article 32 , he contended that the rule was invalid as it placed obstructions on the fundamental right guaranteed under Article 32 to move <ORG> for the enforcement of fundamental rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	This plea was upheld by the majority decision with the result that the order requiring him to furnish security was vacated.
RatioOfTheDecision	In appreciating the effect of this decision, it is necessary to bear in mind the nature of the contentions raised before the <ORG> in that case.
RatioOfTheDecision	The rule itself, in terms, conferred discretion on the court.
RatioOfTheDecision	while dealing with applications made under Article 32 , to impose such terms as to costs as to the giving of security as it thinks fit.
Argument	The learned Solicitor General who supported the validity of the rule, urged that though the order requiring security to be deposited may be said to retard or obstruct the fundamental right of the citizen guaranteed by Article 32(1 ), the rule itself could not be effectively challenged as invalid, because it was merely discretionary; it did not impose an  obligation on the court to demand any security; and he supplemented his argument by contending that under Article 142 of the Constitution, the powers of this court were wide enough to impose any term or condition subject to which proceedings before this <ORG> could be permitted to be conducted.
Argument	He suggested that the powers of this <ORG> under Article 142 were not subject to any of the provisions contained in Part III including Article 32(1).
Argument	On the other hand, Mr. <NAME> who challenged the validity of the rule, urged that though the rule was in form and in substance discretionary, he disputed the validity of the power which the rule conferred on this <ORG> to demand security ..
Argument	It would thus be seen that the main controversy in the case of <NAME> centered round the question as to whether Article 145 conferred powers on this <ORG> to make rules, though they may be inconsistent with the constitutional provisions prescribed by Part III.
Argument	Once it was held that the powers under Article 142 had to be read  subject not only to the fundamental rights, but to other binding statutory provisions, it became clear that the rule which authorised the making of the impugned order was invalid.
Argument	It was in that context that the validity of the order had to be incidentally examined.
Argument	The petition was made not to challenge the order as such, but to challenge the validity of the rule under which the order was made.
Argument	Once a rule was struck down as being invalid, the order passed under the said rule had to be vacated.
Argument	It is difficult to see how this decision can be pressed into service by Mr. <NAME> in support of the argument that a judicial order passed by this <ORG> was held to be subject to the writ jurisdiction of this <ORG> itself .. ".
Argument	In view of this decision in <NAME>'s case (supra) it must be taken as concluded that judicial proceedings in this <ORG> are not subject to the writ jurisdiction thereof.
Argument	On behalf of the appellant,- at one stage, it was contended that the appeal may be taken as a review.
RatioOfTheDecision	Apart from the fa ct that the petition of review had to be filed within 30 days-and here there has been inordinate delay-the petition for review had to be placed before the same <ORG> and now that two of the learned Judges of that Constitution <ORG> are still available, it must have gone only before a <ORG> of five with those two learned Judges.
RatioOfTheDecision	Again under the Rules of the <ORG> a review petition was not to be heard in <ORG> and was liable to be disposed of by circulation.
RatioOfTheDecision	In these circumstances.
RatioOfTheDecision	the petition of appeal could not he taken as a review petition.
RatioOfTheDecision	The question, therefore, to be considered now is what is the modality to be followed for vacating the impugned direction.
RatioOfTheDecision	This being the apex <ORG>, no litigant has any opportunity of approaching any higher forum to question its decisions.
Precedent	Lord <NAME> in 1917 A.C. 170 stated:  "All rules of court are nothing but provisions intended to secure proper administration of justice.
Precedent	It is, therefore, essential that they should be made to serve and be subordinate to that pur pose."
Precedent	This <ORG> in <CITATION_928347> ,  2 SCR 875 reiterated the position by saying:  "Procedure is the handmaid and not a mistress of law, intended to subserve and facilitate the cause of justice and not to govern or obstruct it, like all rules of procedure, this rule demands a construction which would promote this Once judicial satisfaction is reached that the direction was not open to be made and it is accepted as a mistake of the court, it is not only appropriate but also the duty of the <ORG> to rectify the mistake by exercising inherent powers.
Precedent	Judicial opinion heavily leans in favour of this view that a mistake of the <ORG> can be corrected by the <ORG> itself without any fetters.
Precedent	This is on the principle as indicated in <NAME> case (supra).
Precedent	l am of the view that in the present situation, the <ORG>'s inherent powers can be exercised to remedy the mistake.
Precedent	<NAME>, J. speaking for a four-Judge Bench in <NAME> v. <NAME>, [ 1953] SCR 136, at page 153 stated:  "The Judge had juris diction to correct his own error without entering into a discussion of the grounds taken by the decree-holder or the objections raised by the judgment debtors . "
Precedent	<ORG> in Debi v. Habib, ILR 35 All.
Precedent	331, pointed out that an abuse of the process of the <ORG> may be committed by the court or by a party.
Precedent	Where a court employed a procedure in doing something which it never intended to do and there is an abuse of the process of the court it can be corrected.
Precedent	Lord <NAME> spoke for the Law lords thus:  "Quite apart from section 151 , any court might have rightly considered itself to possess an inherent power to rectify the mistake which had been inadvertently made."
Precedent	It was pointed out by <ORG> in <CITATION_467705> , AIR 1916 PC 8:.
Precedent	that:  "Where substantial injustice would othenwise result, the court has, in their Lordships opinion, an inherent power to set aside its own judgments of condemnation so as to let in bona fide claims by parties .. ".
RatioOfTheDecision	Indian authorities are in abundance to support  the view that injustice done should be corrected by applying the principle actus curiae neminem gravabit an act of the court shall prejudice no one.
RatioOfTheDecision	89  To err is human, is the off-quoted saying.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG>s including the apex one are no exception.
RatioOfTheDecision	To own up the mistake when judicial satisfaction is reached does not militate against its status or authority.
RatioOfTheDecision	Perhaps it would enhance both.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is time to sound a note of caution.
RatioOfTheDecision	This <ORG> under its <ORG> ordinarily sits in divisions and not as a whole one.
RatioOfTheDecision	Each <ORG>, whether small or large, exercises the powers vested in the <ORG> and decisions rendered by the <ORG>es irrespective of their size are considered as decisions of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The practice has developed that a larger <ORG> is entitled to overrule the decision of a smaller <ORG> notwithstanding the fact that each of the decisions is that of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	That principle, however, would not apply in the present situation and since we are sitting as a <ORG> of Seven we are not entitled to reverse t he decision of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Overruling when made by a larger <ORG> of an earlier decision of a smaller one is intended to take away the precedent value of the decision without affecting the binding effect of the decision in the particular case.
RulingByPresentCourt	<ORG>, therefore, is not entitled to take advantage of the matter being before a larger <ORG>.
RulingByPresentCourt	In fact, if it is a case of exercise of inherent powers to rectify a mistake it was open even to a five-Judge <ORG> to do that and it did not require a <ORG> larger than <ORG> for that purpose.
RatioOfTheDecision	Mr. <NAME> had told us during arguments that if there was interference in this case there was possibility of litigants thinking that the <ORG> had made a direction by going out of its way because an influential person like <ORG> was involved.
RatioOfTheDecision	We are sorry that such a suggestion was made before us by a senior counsel.
RatioOfTheDecision	If a mistake is detected and the apex <ORG> is not able to correct it with a view to doing justice for fear of being misunderstood, th e cause of justice is bound to suffer and for the apex <ORG> the apprehension would not be a valid consideration.
RatioOfTheDecision	Today it is <NAME> with a political background and perhaps some status and wealth but tomorrow it can be any ill-placed citizen.
RatioOfTheDecision	This <ORG> while administering justice does not take into consideration as to who is before it.
RatioOfTheDecision	Every litigant is entitled to the same consideration and if an order is warranted in the interest of justice, the contention of Mr. <NAME> cannot stand in the way as a bar to the making of that order.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is still another aspect which should be taken note of.
RatioOfTheDecision	Finality of the orders is the rule.
RatioOfTheDecision	By our directing recall of an order the well-settled propositions of law would not be set at naught.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such a situation may not recur in the ordinary course of judicial functioning and if there be one certainly the <ORG> before which it comes would appropriately deal with it.
RatioOfTheDecision	No strait jacket formula can be laid down for judicial functioning particularly for the apex  <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The apprehension that the present decision may be used as a precedent to challenge judicial orders of this <ORG> is perhaps misplaced because those who are familiar with the judicial functioning are aware of the limits and they would not seek support from this case as a precedent.
RatioOfTheDecision	We are sure that if precedent value is sought to be derived out of this decision, the <ORG> which is asked to use this as an instrument would be alive to the peculiar facts and circumstances of the case in which this order is being made.

RatioOfTheDecision	VENKATACHALIAH, <NAME>, a former Chief Minister of Maharashtra, is on trial for certain offences under Sections 161 , 165 , Indian Penal Code and under the Prevention of Corruption Act , 1947.

Precedent	Lord <NAME> said "Finality is a good thing, but justice is a better".
Precedent	[See <ORG>].
Precedent	Considerations of finality are subject to the paramount considerations of justice; but the remedial action must be appropriate and known to law.
Precedent	The question is whether there is any such gross miscarriage of justice in this case, if so whether relief can be granted in the manner now sought.
Precedent	The words of caution of the judicial committee in Venkata Narasimha Appa Row v. The <ORG> of Wards & Ors.
Precedent	[1886] 1 ILR 660 (at page 664) are worth recalling:  92  "There is a salutary maxim which ought to be observed by all courts of last resort-interest reipublicae ut sit finis litium.
Precedent	Its strict observance may occasionally entail hardship upon individual litigants, but the mischief a rising from that source must be small in comparison with the great mischief which would necessarily result from doubt being thrown upon the finality of the decisions of such a tribunal as this."

RatioOfTheDecision	6.
RatioOfTheDecision	Before going into these challenges, it is necessary to say something on the merits of the order under appeal itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	An accused person cannot assert any right to a joint trial with his co-accused.
RatioOfTheDecision	Normally it is the right of the prosecution to decide whom it prosecutes.
RatioOfTheDecision	It can decline to array a person as a co-accused and, instead, examine him as a witness for the prosecution.
RatioOfTheDecision	What weight is to be attached to that evidence, as it may smack of the testimony of a guilty partner, in crime, is a different matter.
RatioOfTheDecision	Prosecution can enter Nolle proseque against any accused-person.
RatioOfTheDecision	It can seek to withdraw a charge against an accused person.
RatioOfTheDecision	These propositions are too well settled to require any further elaboration.
RulingByLowerCourt	Suffice it to say that the matter is concluded by the pronouncement of this <ORG> in <CITATION_563592> ,  2 SCR 624 where <NAME> referred to the argument that the accomplice, a certain Eth <NAME> in that case, had also to be arrayed as an accused and repelled it, observing:  "... Mr. <NAME>'s argument that the Magistrate should have promptly put her in the dock because of her incriminating answers overlooks S. 132 (proviso)".
RulingByLowerCourt	"...
RulingByLowerCourt	The prosecution was not bound to prosecute her, if they thought that her evidence was necessary to break a smugglers' ring.
RulingByLowerCourt	Eth<NAME> was prosecuted by S. 132 (proviso) of the Indian Evidence Act even if she gave evidence incriminating herself.
RulingByLowerCourt	She was a competent witness although her evidence could only be received with the caution necessary in all accomplice evidence ... "  On this point, really, appellant cannot be heard to complain.

Fact	We must now turn to the larger issue raised in the appeal.
RatioOfTheDecision	7 .
RatioOfTheDecision	While <NAME>, learned Senior Counsel for the appel-  99  lant, handling an otherwise delicate and sensitive issue, deployed all the legal tools that a first rate legal-smithy could design, <NAME>, learned Senior Counsel, however, pointed out the impermissibility both as a matter of law and propriety of a different bench embarking upon the present exercise which, in effect, meant the exertion of an appellate and superior jurisdiction over the earlier five Judge Bench and the precedential problems and anomalies such a course would create for the future.
RatioOfTheDecision	8.
RatioOfTheDecision	The contentions raised and urged by <NAME> admit of being summarised and formulated thus:  (a) That <ORG> has, and can, exercise only such jurisdiction as is invested in it by the Constitution and the laws; that even the power under Article 142(1) is not unfettered, but is confined within the ambit of the jurisdiction otherwise available to it; that the <ORG>, like any other court, cannot make any order that viola tes the law; that Section 7(1) of the Criminal Law (Amendment) Act, 1952, (1952 Act) envisages and sets-up a special and exclusive forum for trial of certain offences; that the direction for trial of those offences by a Judge of <ORG> is wholly without jurisdiction and void; and that 'Nullity' of the order could be set up and raised whenever and wherever the order is sought to be enforced or effectuated;  (b) That in directing a Judge of <ORG> to try the case <ORG> virtually sought to create a new jurisdiction and a new forum not existent in and recognised by law and has, accordingly, usurped Legislative powers, violating the basic tenets of the doctrine of separation of powers;  (c) That by being singled out for trial by <ORG>, appellant is exposed to a hostile discrimination, violative of his fundamental rights under Articles 14 and 21 and if the principles in <CITATION_184660633> ,  SCR 284.
RatioOfTheDecision	The law applicable to <NAME> should equally apply to <NAME> ntulay.
RatioOfTheDecision	(d) That the directions for transfer were issued without affording an opportunity to the appellant of being hear,, and therefore void as violative of Rules of Natural Justice.
RatioOfTheDecision	100  (e) That the transfer of the case to <ORG> deprived appellant of an appeal, as of right, to <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	At least one appeal, as of right is the minimal constitutional safeguard.

Precedent	Re: Contentions (a) ar.d (b): In the course of arguments we were treated to a wide ranging, and no less interesting, submissions  on the concept of "jurisdiction" and "nullity" in relation to judicial orders.

RatioOfTheDecision	In relation to the powers of superior courts, the familiar distinction between jurisdictional issues and adjudicatory issues-appropriate to Tribunals of limited jurisdiction,-has no place.
Precedent	Before a superior court there is no distinction in the quality of the decision-maki ng-process respecting jurisdictional questions on the one hand and adjudicatory issues or issues pertaining to the merits, on the other.
Precedent	11.
Precedent	The expression "jurisdiction" or the power to determine is, it is said, a verbal cast of many colours.
Precedent	In the case of a <ORG>, an error of law might become not merely an error in jurisdiction but might partake of the character of an error of jurisdiction.
Precedent	But, otherwise, jurisdiction is a 'legal shelter'-a power to bind despite a possible error in the decision.
Precedent	The existence of jurisdiction does not depend on the correctness of its exercise.
Precedent	The authority to decide embodies a privilege to bind despite error, a privilege which is inherent in and indispensable to every judicial function.
Precedent	The characteristic attribute of a judicial act is that it binds whether it be right or it be wrong.
Precedent	In <NAME> v. <NAME>, [1900] 27 I.A.
Precedent	216 the executing <ORG> had quite wrongly, held that a particular person represented the estate of the deceased Judgment-debtor and put the prope rty for sale in execution.
Precedent	The judicial committee said:  "In doing so, the <ORG> was exercising its jurisdiction.
Precedent	It made a sad mistake, it is true; but a court has jurisdiction to decide wrong as well as right.
Precedent	If it decides wrong, the wronged party can only take the course prescribed by law for setting matters right and if that course is not taken the decision, however wrong.
Precedent	cannot be disturbed."
Precedent	In the course of the arguments there were references to the Anisminic case.
Precedent	In my view, reliance on the Anisminic principle is wholly misplaced in this case.
Precedent	That case related to the powers of Tribunals of limited jurisdiction.
Precedent	It would be a mistake of first magnitude to import these inhibitions as to jurisdiction into the concept of the jurisdiction of superior courts.
Precedent	A finding of a superior court even on a question of its own jurisdiction, however grossly erroneous it may, otherwise be, is not a nullity; nor one which could at all be said to have been reached without jurisdiction, susceptible to be ignored or  to admit of any collateral-attack.
Precedent	Otherwise, the adjudications of superior courts would be held-up to ridicule and the remedies generally arising from and considered concomitants of such classification of judicial-errors would be so seriously abused and expanded as to make a mockery of those foundational principles essential to the stability of administration of justice.
Precedent	The superior court has jurisdiction to determine its own jurisdiction and an error in that determination does not make it an error of jurisdiction.

" .. Precedent	It is only where it can be shown lawfully, that some matter or thing essential to jurisdiction is wanting, that the proceeding is void, collaterally.

RatioOfTheDecision	(emphasis supplied) Reliance by <NAME> on these principles to support his submission that the power under Section 407 was unaffected and that the decision in State of Rajasthan v.  <NAME> (supra), can not also be taken to have concluded the matter, is not un-arguable.
RatioOfTheDecision	I would, therefore, hold contentions (a) and (b) against appellant.
Precedent	19 Re: contention (c):  The fundamenta l right under Article 14 , by all reckoning, has a very high place in constitutional scale of values.
Precedent	Before a person is deprived of his personal liberty, not only that the Procedure established by law must strictly be complied with and not departed from to the disadvantage or detriment of the person but also that the procedure for such deprivation of personal liberty must be reasonable, fair and just.
Precedent	Article 21 imposes limitations upon the procedure and requires it to conform to such standards of reasonableness, fairness and justness as the <ORG> acting as sentinel of fundamental rights would in the context, consider necessary and requisite.
Precedent	The court will be the arbiter of the question whether the procedure is reasonable, fair and just.
Precedent	If the operation of Section 407 , Cr.P.C .
Precedent	is not impliedly excluded and therefore, enables the withdrawal of a case by the High <ORG> to itself for trial as, indeed, has been held by the earlier bench, the argument based on Article 14 would really amount to a challenge to  the very vires of Section 407 .
Precedent	All accused persons cannot claim to be tried by the same Judge.
Precedent	The discriminations-inherent in the choice of one of the concurrent jurisdictions-are not brought about by an inanimate statutory-rule or by executive fiat.
Precedent	The withdrawal of a case under Section 407 is made by a conscious judicial act and is the result of judicial discernment.
Precedent	If the law permits the withdrawal of the trial to <ORG> from a Special Judge, such a law enabling withdrawal would not, prima facie, be bad as violation of Article 14.
Precedent	The five Judge bench in the earlier case has held that such a transfer is permissible under law.
Precedent	The appeal to the principle in <NAME> case (supra), in such a context would be somewhat out of place.
Precedent	If the law did not permit such a transfer then the trial before a forum which is not according to law violates the rights of the accused person.
Precedent	In the earlier decision the transfer has been held to be permissible.
RatioOfTheDecision	That decision has assumed finality.
RatioOfTheDecision	If appe llant says that he is singled out for a hostile treatment on the ground alone that he is exposed to a trial before a Judge of the .
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> then the submission has a touch of irony.
RatioOfTheDecision	Indeed that a trial by a Judge of the <ORG> makes for added re-assurance of justice, has been recognised in a number of judicial pronouncement.

RatioOfTheDecision	A judge of the <ORG> hears appeals arising from the decisions of the Special Judge, and exercises a jurisdiction which includes powers co-extensive with that of the trial court.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is, thus, no substance in contention (c).

RatioOfTheDecision	Once it as held that the powers under Article 142 had to be read subject not only to the fundamental rights, but to other binding statutory provisions, it became clear that the ru ler which authorised the making of the impugned order was invalid.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was in that context that the validity of the order had to be incidentally examined.
Precedent	The petition was A made not to challenge the order as such, but to challenge the validity of the rule under which the order was made Repelling the contention learned Chief Justice said:  "...
Precedent	It is difficult to see now this decision can be pressed into service by Mr. <NAME> in support of the argument that a judicial order passed by this <ORG> was held to be subject to the writ jurisdiction of this <ORG> itself .. "  A passage from <NAME> & <NAME> "Discretion to Disobey", 1973 Edn.
Precedent	may usefully by recalled:  "on one view, it would appear that the right of a citizen to defy illegitimate judicial authority should be the same as his right to defy illegitimate legislative authority.
Precedent	After all, if a rule that transgresses the Constitution or is otherwise invalid is no law at all and never was one, it should hardly matter whether a court or a legislature made the  rule.
Precedent	Yet the prevailing approach of the courts has been to treat invalid court orders quite differently from invalid statutes.
Precedent	The long established principle of the old equity courts was that an erroneously issued injunction must be obeyed until the error was judicially determined.
Precedent	Only where the issuing court could be said to have lacked jurisdiction in the sense of authority to adjudicate the cause and to reach the parties through its mandate were disobedient contemnors permitted to raise the invalidity of the order as a full defence.
Precedent	By and large, American courts have declined to treat the unconstitutionality of a court order as a jurisdictional defect within this traditional equity principle, and in notable instances they have qualified that principle even where the defect was jurisdiction in the accepted sense."
Precedent	(See 111).
Precedent	Indeed <NAME>, J. in his opinion rejected the contention of the appellant in these terms:  "In view of this decision in <NAME>'s case, supra, it must be taken as conclude d that judicial proceedings in this <ORG> are not subject to the writ jurisdiction thereof."
RatioOfTheDecision	119  There is no substance in contention (f) either.
RatioOfTheDecision	A  24.
RatioOfTheDecision	Contention (g):  It is asserted that the impugned directions issued by the five Judge Bench was per-incuriam as it ignored the Statute and the earlier <NAME>'s case.
RatioOfTheDecision	B But the point is that the circumstance that a decision is reached per-incuriam, merely serves to denude the decision of its precedent value.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such a decision would not be binding as a judicial precedent.
RatioOfTheDecision	A co-ordinate bench can disagree with it and decline to follow it.
RatioOfTheDecision	A larger bench can over rule such decision.
Precedent	When a previous decision is so overruled it does not happen-nor has the overruling bench any jurisdiction so to do-that the finality of the operative order, inter-parties, in the previous decision is overturned.
Precedent	In this context the word 'decision' means only the reason for the previous order and not the operative- order in the previous decision, binding inter-parties.
Precedent	Even if a previou s decision is overruled by a larger-bench, the efficacy and binding nature, of the adjudication expressed in the operative order remains undisturbed inter-parties.
Precedent	Even if the earlier decision of the five Judge bench is per- incuriam the operative part of the order cannot be interfered within the manner now sought to be done.
Precedent	That apart the five Judge bench gave its reason.
Precedent	The reason, in our opinion, may or may not be sufficient.
Precedent	There is advertence to Section 7(1) of the 1952 Act and to the exclusive jurisdiction created thereunder.
Precedent	There is also reference to Section 407 of the Criminal Procedure Code.
Precedent	Can such a decision be characterised as one reached per- incurium?
Precedent	Indeed, <NAME>, J. says this on the point:  "Overruling when made by a larger bench of an earlier decision of a smaller one is intended to take away the precedent value of the decision without affecting the binding effect of the decision in the particular case.
Precedent	<ORG>, therefore, is not entitled to take advantage of the matter being  before a larger bench .. "  I respectfully agree.
Precedent	Point (g) is bereft of substance and merits.
Precedent	25.
Precedent	Re: Contention (h):  The argument is that the appellant has been prejudiced by a mistake of the <ORG> and it is not only within power but a duty as well, H of the <ORG> to correct its own mistake, so that no party is prejudiced by the <ORG>'s mistake: Actus Curiae Neminem Gravabid.
Precedent	I am afraid this maxim has no application to conscious conclusions reached in a judicial decision.
Precedent	The maxim is not a'source of a general power to reopen and rehear adjudication which have otherwise assumed finality.
Precedent	The maxim operates in a different and narrow area.
Precedent	The best illustration of the operation of the maxim is provided by the application of the rule of nunc-pro-tunc.
Precedent	For instance, if owing to the delay in what the court should, otherwise, have done earlier but did later, a party suffers owing to events occurring in the interrugnum, the <ORG> has the power to remedy it.
Precedent	The area of operation of the maxim is.
Precedent	generally, pro cedural.
Precedent	Errors in judicial findings, either of facts or law or operative decisions consciously arrived at as a part of the judicial-exercise cannot be interfered with by resort to his maxim.
Precedent	There is no substance in contention (h).
Precedent	26. lt is true that the highest court in the land should no., by technicalities of procedure forge fetters on its own feet and disable itself in cases of serious miscarriages of justice.
Precedent	It is said that "Life of law is not logic; it has been experience."
Precedent	But it is equally true as <NAME> said: But <NAME> did not tell us that logic is to be ignored when experience is silent.
Precedent	Those who do not put the teachings of experience and the lessons of logic out of consideration would tell what inspires confidence in the judiciary and what does not.
Precedent	Judicial vacillations fall in the latter category and undermine respect of the judiciary and judicial institutions, denuding thereby respect for law and the confidence in the even-handedness in the administrating of justice by <ORG>.
Precedent	It would be  gross injustice, says an author, (Miller-'data of jurisprudence') to decide alternate cases on opposite principles.
Precedent	The power to alter a decision by review must be expressly conferred or necessarily inferred.
Precedent	The power of review-and the limitations on the power-under Article 137 are implicit recognitions of what would, otherwise, be final and irrevocable.
Precedent	No appeal could be made to the doctrine of inherent powers of the <ORG> either.
Precedent	Inherent powers do not confer, or constitute a source of, jurisdiction.
Precedent	They are to be exercised in aid of a jurisdiction that is already invested.
Precedent	The remedy of the appellant, if any, is recourse to Article 137; no where else.
Precedent	This appears to me both good sense and good law.
Precedent	The appeal is dismissed.
Precedent	<NAME>, J.
Precedent	1.
Precedent	I have had the benefit of perusing the drafts of the judgments proposed by my learned brothers <NAME>, <NAME> and <NAME>, JJ.

Precedent	THE ISSUES  1.
Precedent	This is an appeal by special leave from a judgment of <NAME>, of <ORG>.
Precedent	The appellant is being tried for offences under Ss.
Precedent	120B, 420, 161 and 165 of the Indian Penal Code ( I.P.C .)
Precedent	read with S. 5(1)(d) and 5(2) of the Prevention of Corruption Act, 1947.
Precedent	The proceedings against the appellant were started in the <ORG> of <NAME>, a Special Judge, appointed under S. 6(1) of the Criminal Law (Amendment) Act, 1952 (hereinafter referred to as 'the 1952 Act').
Precedent	The proceedings have had a chequered career as narrated in the judgment of my learned brother <NAME>, J.
Precedent	Various issues have come up for consideration of this <ORG> at the earlier stages of the proceedings and the judgments of this <ORG> ha ve been reported In 1982 2 S.C.C.
Precedent	463, 1984 2 SCR 495, 1984 2 SCR 914, 1984 3 SCR 412, 1984 3 SCR 482 and 1986 2 S.C.C.
Precedent	716.
RatioOfTheDecision	At present the appellant is being tried by a learned <NAME>dge of <ORG> nominated by the Chief <NAME>stice of <ORG> in pursuance of the direction given by this Court in its order dated 16.2.1984 (reported in 1984 2 SCR 495).
RatioOfTheDecision	By the order presently under appeal, the learned <NAME>dge (s) framed as many as 79 charges against the appellant and (b) rejected the prayer of the appellant that certain persons, named as co-conspirators of the appellant in the complaint on the basis of which the prosecution has been launched should be arrayed as co-accused along with him.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the principal contention urged on behalf of the appellant before us centres not round the merits of the order under appeal on the above two issues but round what the counsel for the appellant has described as a fundamental and far- reaching objection to the very validity of his trial before the learned <NAME> dge.
RatioOfTheDecision	As already stated, the trial is being conducted by the learned Judge pursuant to the direction of this <ORG> dated 16.2.1984.
RatioOfTheDecision	The contention of the learned counsel is that the said direction is per incuriam, illegal, invalid, contrary to the principles of natural justice and violative of the fundamental rights of the petitioner.
RatioOfTheDecision	This naturally raises two important issues for our consideration:  A.
RatioOfTheDecision	Whether the said direction is inoperative, invalid or illegal, as alleged; and B.
RatioOfTheDecision	Whether, if it is, this <ORG> can and should recall, withdraw, revoke or set aside the same in the present proceedings.
RatioOfTheDecision	Since the issues involve a review or reconsideration of a direction given by a <ORG> of five judges of this <ORG>, this seven-judge <ORG> has been constituted to hear the appeal.
RatioOfTheDecision	2.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is not easy to say which of the two issues raised should be touched upon first as, whichever one is taken up first, the second will not arise for consideration unless the first is answered in the affirmative.
RatioOfTheDecision	However, as the corr ectness of the direction issued is impugned by the petitioner, as there is no detailed discussion in the earlier order on the points raised by the petitioner, and as <NAME>, J. has expressed an opinion on these contentions with parts of which I am unable to agree, it will be perhaps more convenient to have a look at the first issue as if it were coming up for consideration for the first time before us and then, depending upon the answer to it, consider the second issue as to whether this Court has any jurisdiction to recall or revoke the earlier order.
RatioOfTheDecision	The issues will, therefore, be discussed in this order.
Precedent	A.
Precedent	ARE THE DIRECTIONS ON 16.2.1984 PROPER, VALID AND LEGAL?
Precedent	3.
Precedent	For the appellant, it is contended that the direction given in the last para of the order of the Bench of five Judges dated 16.2.1984 (extracted in the judgment of <NAME>, J.)
Precedent	is vitiated by illegality, irregularity and lack of jurisdiction on the following grounds:  (i) Conferment of jurisdiction on courts is the fu nction of the legislature.
Precedent	It was not competent for this <ORG> to confer jurisdiction on a learned Judge of the High <ORG> to try the appellant, as, under the 1952 Act, an offence of the type in question can be tried only by a special Judge appointed thereunder.
RatioOfTheDecision	This has been overlooked while issuing the direction which is, therefore, per incuriam.
RatioOfTheDecision	(ii) The direction above-mentioned (a) relates to an issue which was not before the <ORG> (b) on which no arguments were addressed and (c) in regard to which the appellant had no opportunity to make his submissions.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was nobody's case before the above <ORG> that the trial of the accused should no longer be conducted by a Special Judge but should be before a High <ORG> Judge.
RatioOfTheDecision	(iii) In issuing the impugned direction, the <ORG> violated the principles of natural justice, as mentioned above.
RatioOfTheDecision	It also overlooked that, as a result thereof, the petitioner (a) was discriminated against by being put to trial before a different forum as compared to other public servants a ccused of similar offences and (b) lost valuable rights of revision and first appeal to <ORG> which he would have had, if tried in the normal course.
Precedent	The direction was thus also violative of natural justice as well as the fundamental rights of the petitioner under Article 14 and 21 of the Constitution.
Precedent	Primary Jurisdiction  4.
Precedent	There can be-and, indeed, counsel for the respondent had-no quarrel with the initial premise of the learned counsel for the appellant that the conferment of jurisdiction on courts is a matter for the legislature.
Precedent	Entry 77 of List I, entry 3 of List II and entries 1, 2, 11A and 46 of List III of the Seventh Schedule of the Constitution set out the respective powers of parliament and the State <ORG> in that regard.
Precedent	It is common ground that the jurisdiction to try offences of the type with which are concerned here is vested by the 1952 Act in Special Judges appointed by the respective State Governments.
Precedent	The first question that has been agitated before us is whether this C ourt was right in transferring the case for trial from the Court of a Special Judge, to a Judge nominated by the Chief Justice of Bombay.
Precedent	<ORG>  5.
Precedent	The power of <ORG> to transfer cases can be traced, in criminal matters, either to Art.
Precedent	139A of the Constitution or Section 406 of the Code of Criminal Procedure ("Cr.
Precedent	P.C.
RatioOfTheDecision	), 1973.
RatioOfTheDecision	Here, again, it is common ground that neither of these provisions cover the present case.
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME>, learned counsel for the respondent, seeks to support the order of transfer by reference to Section 407 (not Section 406 ) of the Code and cl.
Precedent	29 of the Letters Patent of the Bombay High Court.
Precedent	Section 407 reads thus:  (1) Whenever it is made to appear to <ORG>  124  (a) that a fair and impartial inquiry or trial cannot be had in any <ORG> subordinate thereto, or  (b) that some question of law of unusual difficulty is likely to arise, or  (c) that an order under this section is required by any provision of this Code, or will te nd to the general convenience of the parties or witnesses, or is expedient for the ends of justice, it may order-  (i) that any offence be inquired into or tried by any <ORG> not qualified under Section 177 to 185 (both inclusive), but in other respects competent to inquire into or try such offences;  (ii) that any particular case or appeal, or class of cases or appeals, be transferred from a Criminal <ORG> subordinate to its authority to any other such Criminal <ORG> of equal or superior jurisdiction;  (iii) that any particular case be committed for trial to a <ORG> of Session; or  (iv) that any particular case or appeal be transferred to and tried before itself.
Precedent	(2) the High <ORG> may act either on the report of the lower court or on the application of a party interested or on its own initiative:  XXX XXX XXX XXX XXX XXX XXX XXX XXX  (9) Nothing in this section shall be deemed to affect any order of Government under Section 197 ."
Precedent	And cl.
Precedent	29 of the Letters Patent of the Bombay High <ORG> runs thus:  "And  we do further ordain that the said <ORG> shall have power to direct the transfer of any criminal case or appeal from any <ORG> to any other <ORG> of appeal or superior jurisdiction, and also to direct the preliminary investigation of trial of any criminal case by any officer of <ORG> otherwise competent to investigate or try it though such case belongs, in ordinary course, to the jurisdiction of some other officer, of <ORG>."
RatioOfTheDecision	The argument is that this power of transfer vested in the <ORG> can well be exercised by the Supreme <ORG> while dealing with an appeal from the <ORG> in the case.
RatioOfTheDecision	6.
Argument	For the appellant, it is contended that the power of transfer under section 407 cannot be invoked to transfer a case from a Special Judge appointed under the 1952 Act to the <ORG>.
Argument	Learned counsel for the appellant contends that the language of section 7(1) of the Act is mandatory; it directs that offences specified in the Act can be tried only by persons appointed, under S. 6(2) of the Act, by the Sta te Government, to be special judges, No other Judge, it is said, has jurisdiction to try such a case, even if he is a Judge of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this context, it is pointed out that a person, to be appointed as a special Judge, under section 6(2) of the 1952 Act, should be one who is, or has been, a <NAME> Judge (which expression in this context includes an Additional <NAME> Judge and/or an Assistant <NAME> Judge).
RatioOfTheDecision	All <ORG> Judges may not have been <NAME> Judges earlier and, it is common ground, <NAME>, J. who has been nominated by the Chief Justice for trying this case does not fulfill the qualifications prescribed for appointment as a Special Judge.
RatioOfTheDecision	But, that consideration apart, the argument is that, while a <ORG> can transfer a case from one special judge to another, and <ORG>, from a special judge in one State to a special judge in another State, a <ORG> cannot withdraw a case from a Special Judge to itself and <ORG> cannot transfer a case from a Special Jud ge to <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	7.
RatioOfTheDecision	On the other hand, it is contended for the respondent that the only purpose of the 1952 Act is to ensure that cases of corruption and bribery do not get bogged up in the ordinary criminal courts which are over- burdened with all sorts of cases.
RatioOfTheDecision	Its object is not to create special courts in the sense of courts manned by specially qualified personnel or courts following any special type of procedure.
RatioOfTheDecision	All that is done is to earmark some of the existing sessions judges for trying these offences exclusively.
RatioOfTheDecision	The idea is just to segregate corruption and bribery cases to a few of the sessions judges so that they could deal with them effectively and expeditiously.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is a classification in which the emphasis is on the types of offences and nature of offenders rather than on the qualifications of judges.
RatioOfTheDecision	That being so, the requirement in section 7(1) that these cases should be tried by special judges only is intended just to exclude their trial by the other normal criminal courts of coordinat e jurisdiction and not to exclude <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	8.
RatioOfTheDecision	Before dealing with these contentions, it may be useful to touch upon the question whether a judge of <ORG> can be appointed by <ORG> as a special judge to try offences of the type specified in section 6 of the 1952 Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	It will be seen at once that not all the judges of <ORG> (but only those elevated from the <ORG> subordinate judiciary) would fulfill the qualifications prescribed under section 6(2) of the 1952 Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	Though there is nothing in ss.
RatioOfTheDecision	6 and 7 read together to preclude altogether the appointment of a judge of <ORG> fulfilling the above qualifications as a special judge, it would appear that such is not the (atleast not the normal) contemplation of the Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	Perhaps it is possible to argue that, under the Act, it is permissible for <ORG> to appoint one of <ORG> Judges (who has been a Sessions Judge) to be a Special Judge under the Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	If that had been done, that Judge would have bee n a Special Judge and would have been exercising his original jurisdiction in conducting the trial.
RatioOfTheDecision	But that is not the case here.
Precedent	In response to a specific question put by us as to whether a <ORG> Judge can be appointed as a Special Judge under the 1952 Act, <NAME> submitted that a <ORG> Judge cannot be so appointed.
RatioOfTheDecision	I am inclined to agree.
Precedent	The scheme of the Act, in particular the provision contained in ss.
Precedent	8(3A) and 9 , militate against this concept.
RatioOfTheDecision	Hence, apart from the fact that in this case no appointment of a <ORG> Judge, as a Special Judge, has in fact been made, it is not possible to take the view that the statutory provisions permit the conferment of a jurisdiction to try this case on a <ORG> Judge as a Special Judge.
RatioOfTheDecision	9.
RatioOfTheDecision	Turning now to the powers of transfer under section 407 , one may first deal with the decision of this <ORG> in <CITATION_351344> ,  2 S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	678 on which both counsel strongly relied.
RatioOfTheDecision	That was a decision by three judges of this <ORG> on a petition unde r section 527 of the 1898 Cr.P.C .
RatioOfTheDecision	(corresponding to section 406 of the 1973 Cr.P.C .
RatioOfTheDecision	).
RatioOfTheDecision	The petitioner had prayed for the transfer of a case pending in the court of a Special Judge in Bharatpur, Rajasthan to another criminal court of equal or superior jurisdiction subordinate to <ORG> other than <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The petition was eventually dismissed on merits.
RatioOfTheDecision	But <ORG> dealt with the provisions of section 527 of the 1898 A Cr.P.C .
Argument	in the context of an objection taken by the respondent State that <ORG> did not have the jurisdiction to transfer a case pending before the Special Judge, Bharatpur.
Precedent	The contention was that a case assigned by the State Government under the 1952 Act to a Special Judge cannot be transferred at all because, under the terms of that Act, which is a self-contained special law, such a case must be tried only by the designated Special Judge.
Precedent	The Court observed that the argument was extremely plausible but not capable of bearing close scrutiny.
Precedent	Af ter referring to the provisions of section 6 , 7 and 8 of the 1952 Act, the <ORG> set out the arguments for the <ORG> thus:  "The Advocate-General, Rajasthan, in opposing the petition relies principally on the provisions of section 7(1) and 7(2) and contends that the two sub-sections create two restrictions which must be read together.
Precedent	The first is that offences specified in section 6(1) can be tried by Special Judges only.
Precedent	The second is that every such offence shall be tried by the Special Judge for the area within which it is committed and if there are more special judges in that area by the Special Judge chosen by the Government.
Precedent	These two conditions, being statutory, it is submitted that no order can be made under section 527 because, on transfer, even if a special judge is entrusted with the case, the second condition is bound to be broken."
Precedent	Dealing with this contention the <ORG> observed:  "This condition, if literally understood, would lead to the conclusion that a case once made over to a special Jud ge in an area where there is no other special Judge, cannot be transferred at all.
RatioOfTheDecision	This could hardly have been intended.
RatioOfTheDecision	If this were so, the power to transfer a case intra-state under s. 526 of the Code of Criminal Procedure, on a parity of reasoning, must also be lacking.
Precedent	But this <ORG> in <NAME> v. State of Bihar,  2 S.C.R.
Precedent	50 unheld the transfer of a case by the High <ORG> which took it to a special judge who had no jurisdiction in the area where the offence was committed.
Precedent	In holding that the transfer was valid this <ORG> relied upon the third sub-section of Section 8 of the Act.
Precedent	That sub-section preserves the application of any provision of the Code of Criminal Procedure it it is not inconsistent with the Act, save as provided in the first two sub-sections of that section.
Precedent	The question, therefore, resolves itself to this: Is there an inconsistency between S. 527 of the Code and the second sub-section of S. 7 ?
Precedent	The answer is that there is none.
Precedent	Apparently this <ORG> in the earlier case found n o inconsistency and the reasons appear to be there: The condition that an offence specified in S. 6(2) shall be tried by a special Judge for the area within which it is committed merely specifies which of several special Judges appointed in the State by <ORG> shall try it.
Precedent	The provision is analogous to others under which the jurisdiction of Magistrates and Sessions Judges is deter mined on a territorial basis.
Precedent	Enactments in the Code of Criminal Procedure intended to confer territorial jurisdiction upon courts and Presiding officers have never been held to stand in the way of transfer of criminal cases outside those areas of territorial jurisdiction.
RatioOfTheDecision	The order of transfer when it is made under the powers given by the Code invests another officer with jurisdiction although ordinarily he would lack territorial jurisdiction to try the case.
RatioOfTheDecision	The order of this <ORG>, therefore, which transfer(s) a case from one special Judge subordinate to one High <ORG> to another special Judge subordinate to anothe <ORG> creates jurisdiction in the latter in much the same way as the transfer by <ORG> from one Sessions Judge in a Session Division to another Sessions Judge in another Sessions Division.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is no comparison between the first sub- section and the second sub-section of Section 7 .
RatioOfTheDecision	The condition in the second sub-section of S. 7 is not of the same character as the condition in the first sub-section.
Precedent	The first sub-section creates a condition which is a sine qua non for the trial of certain offences.
Precedent	That condition is that the trial must be before a special Judge.
Precedent	The second sub- section distributes the work between special Judges and lays emphasis on the fact that trial must be before a special Judge appointed for the area in which the offence is committed.
Precedent	This second condition is on a par with the distribution of work territorially between different Sessions Judges and Magistrates.
Precedent	An order of transfer, by the very nature of things must, some times, result in taking the case out of the ter ritory and the provisions of the Code which are preserved by the third sub-  129  section of S. 8 must supervene to enable this to be done and the second sub-section of S. 7 must yield.
RatioOfTheDecision	We do not consider that this creates any inconsistency because the territorial jurisdiction created by the second sub-section of S. 7 operates in a different sphere and under different circumstances.
RatioOfTheDecision	Inconsistency can only be found if two provisions of law apply in identical circumstances and create contradictions.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such a situation does not arise when either this <ORG> or the High <ORG> exercises its powers of transfer.
Precedent	We are accordingly of the opinion that the Supreme <ORG> in exercise of its jurisdiction and power under S. 527 of the Code of Criminal Procedure can transfer a case from a Special Judge subordinate to the High <ORG> to another special Judge subordinate to another High <ORG>. "
RatioOfTheDecision	(emphasis added)  10.
RatioOfTheDecision	The attempt of <NAME> is to bring the present case within the scope of the observations contained in the  latter part of the extract set out above.
RatioOfTheDecision	He submits that a special judge, except insofar as a specific provision to the contrary is made, is a court subordinate to <ORG>, as explained in 1984 2 S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	914 (at pages 943-4) and proceedings before him are subject to the provisions of the 1973 Cr.P.C .
RatioOfTheDecision	; the field of operation of the first sub- section of section 7 is merely to earmark certain Sessions Judges for purposes of trying cases of corruption by public servants and this provision is, in principle, not different from the earmarking of cases on the basis of territorial jurisdiction dealt with by sub- section 2 of section 7 .
RatioOfTheDecision	The argument is no doubt a plausible one.
RatioOfTheDecision	It does look somewhat odd to say that a Sessions Judge can, but a <ORG> Judge cannot, try an offence under the Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	The object of the Act, as rightly pointed out by counsel, is only to segregate certain cases to special courts which will concentrate on such cases so as to expedite their disposal and not to oust the superior juris diction of <ORG> or its powers of superintendencet over subordinate courts under article 227 of the Constitution, an aspect only of which is reflected in s. 407 of the Cr.P.C .
RatioOfTheDecision	However, were the matter to be considered as res integra, I would be inclined to accept the contention urged on behalf of the appellant, for the following reasons.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the first place, the argument of the counsel for the respondent runs counter to the observations made by <ORG> in the earlier part of the extract set out above that the first sub-section of section 7 and the second sub-section are totally different in character.
RatioOfTheDecision	The first sub-section deals with a sine qua non for the trial of certain offences, whereas the second sub-section is only of a pro-  130  cedural nature earmarking territorial jurisdiction among persons competent to try the offence.
RatioOfTheDecision	They are, therefore, vitally different in nature.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> has clearly held in the passage extracted above that the case can be transferred only from one  special judge to another.
RatioOfTheDecision	In other words, while the requirement of territorial jurisdiction is subordinate to S. 406 or 407, the requirement that the trial should be by a special judge is not.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is true that those observations are not binding on this larger <ORG> and moreover <ORG> there was dealing only with an objection based on sub-section (2) of Section 7 .
RatioOfTheDecision	It is, however, clear that the <ORG>, even if it had accepted the transfer petition of <NAME>, would have rejected a prayer to transfer the case to a court other than that of a Special Judge appointed by the transferee State.
RatioOfTheDecision	I am in respectful agreement with the view taken in that case that there is a vital qualitative difference between the two sub-sections and that while a case can be transferred to a special judge who may not have the ordinary territorial jurisdiction over it, a transfer cannot be made to an ordinary magistrate or a court of session even if it has territorial jurisdiction.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the contention of the learned  counsel for the respondent that s. 7(1) and s. 407 operate in different fields and are not inconsistent with each other were right, it should be logically possible to say that <ORG> can, under s. 407 , transfer a case from a special judge to any other <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	But such a conclusion would be clearly repugnant to the scheme of the 1952 Act and plainly incorrect.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is, therefore, difficult to accept the argument of <NAME> that we should place the restriction contained in the first sub-section of section 7 also as being on the same footing as that in the second sub- section and hold that the power of transfer contained in the Criminal Procedure Code can be availed of to transfer a case from a Special Judge to any other criminal court or even <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The case can be transferred only from one special judge to another special judge; it cannot be transferred even to a <ORG> Judge except where a <ORG> Judge is appointed as a Special Judge.
RatioOfTheDecision	A power of transfer postulates th at the court to which transfer or withdrawal is sought is competent to exercise jurisdiction over the case.
RatioOfTheDecision	(vide, <ORG> v. <NAME>,  2 S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	800).
RatioOfTheDecision	11.
RatioOfTheDecision	This view also derives support from two provisions of S. 407 itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	The first is this.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even when a case is transferred from one criminal court to another, the restriction as to territorial jurisdiction may be infringed.
RatioOfTheDecision	To obviate a contention based on lack of territorial jurisdiction in the transferee court in such a case, clause  (ii) of s. 407 provides that the order of transfer will prevail, lack of jurisdiction under Ss.
RatioOfTheDecision	177 to 185 of the Code notwithstanding.
RatioOfTheDecision	The second difficulty arises, even under the Cr.P.C .
RatioOfTheDecision	itself, by virtue of S. 197 which not only places restriction on the institution of certain prosecutions against public servants without <ORG> sanction but also empowers the <ORG>, inter alia, to determine the court before which such trial is to be conducted.
RatioOfTheDecision	When the forum of such a trial is transferred under s. 407 an  objection may be taken to the continuance of the trial by the transferee court based on the order passed under s. 197 .
RatioOfTheDecision	This eventuality is provided against by s. 407(9) of the Act which porvides that nothing in s. 407 shall be deemed to affect an order passed under s. 407 .
RatioOfTheDecision	Although specifically providing for these contingencies, the section is silent in so far as a transfer from the court of a Special Judge under the 1952 Act is concerned though it is a much later enactment.
RatioOfTheDecision	12.
RatioOfTheDecision	On the contrary, the language of s. 7(1) of the 1952 Act places a definite hurdle in the way of construing s. 407 of the Cr.P.C .
RatioOfTheDecision	as overriding its provisions.
RatioOfTheDecision	For, it opens with the words:  "Notwithstanding anything contained in the Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898 or in any other law".
RatioOfTheDecision	In view of this non-obstanti clause also, it becomes difficult to hold that the provisions of section 407 of the 1973 Cr.P.C .
RatioOfTheDecision	will override, or even operate consistently with, the provisions of the 1952 Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	For the same reason it is not possible  to hold that the power of transfer contained in clause 29 of the Letters Patent of the Bombay High Court can be exercised in a manner not contemplated by section 7(1) of the 1952 Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	13.
