RatioOfTheDecision	Thirdly, whatever may be the position where a case is transferred from one special judge to another or from one ordinary subordirate criminal court to another of equal or superior jurisdiction, the withdrawal of a case by <ORG> from such a Court to itself for trial places certain handicaps on the accused.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is true that the court to which the case has been transferred is a superior court and in fact, <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Unfortunately, however, the high Court judge is not a person to whom the trial of the case can be assigned under s. 7(1) of the 1952 Act.
Precedent	As pointed out by <ORG> in <NAME> v. <NAME>, [1955] 1 S.C.R.
Precedent	448 at pp.
RatioOfTheDecision	464 in a slightly different context, the circumstance that a much superior forum is assigned to try a case than the one normally available cannot by itself be trea ted as a "sufficient safeguard and a good substitute" for the normal forum and the rights available under the normal procedure.
RatioOfTheDecision	The accused here loses his right of coming up in revision or appeal to <ORG> from the interlocutory and final orders of the trial court.
RatioOfTheDecision	He loses the right of having two courts-a subordinate court and <ORG>- adjudicate upon his contentions before bringing the matter up in <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Though, as is pointed out later, these are not such handicaps as violate the fundamental rights of such an accused, they are circumstances which create prejudice to the accused and may not be overlooked in adopting one construction of the statute in preference to the other.
RatioOfTheDecision	14.
Argument	<NAME> vehemently contended that the construction of s. 407 sought for by the appellant is totally opposed to well settled canons of statutory construction.
Argument	He urged that the provisions of the 1952 Act should be interpreted in the light of the objects it sought to achieve and its amplitude should  not be extended beyond its limited objective.
Argument	He said that a construction of the Act which leads to repugnancy with, or entails pro tanto repeal of, the basic criminal procedural law and seeks to divest jurisdiction vested in a superior court should be avoided.
RatioOfTheDecision	These aspects have been considered earlier.
RatioOfTheDecision	The 1952 Act sought to expedite the trial of cases involving public servants by the creation of courts presided over by experienced special judges to be appointed by <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is however nothing implausible in saying that the Act having already earmarked these cases for trial by experienced <ORG> Judges made this provision immune against the applicability of the provisions of other laws in general and the Cr.P.C .
RatioOfTheDecision	in particular.
RatioOfTheDecision	Effect is only being given to these express and specific words used in section 7(1) and no question arises of any construction being encouraged that is repugnant to the Cr.P.C .
RatioOfTheDecision	Or involves an implied repeal, pro tanto, of its provisions.
RatioOfTheDecision	As has already been point ed out, if the requirement in s. 7(1) were held to be subordinate to the provisions contained in s. 406-7 , then in principle, even a case falling under the 1952 Act can be transferred to any other Sessions Judge and that would defeat the whole purpose of the Act and is clearly not envisaged by it.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> power of transfer  15.
RatioOfTheDecision	It will have been noticed that the power of transfer under section 407 or cl.
RatioOfTheDecision	29 of the Letters Patent which has been discussed above is a power vested in <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	So the question will arise whether, even assuming that <ORG> could have exercised such power, <ORG> could have done so.
RatioOfTheDecision	On behalf of the respondent, it was contended that, as the power of <ORG> under s. 407 can be exercised on application of a party or even suo motu and can be exercised by it at any stage irrespective of whether any application or matter in connection with the case is pending before it or not, <ORG>, as an appellate Court, has a co-equal jurisdiction  to exercise the power of transfer in the same manner as <ORG> could.
RatioOfTheDecision	In any event, <ORG> could exercise the power as one incidental or ancillary to the power of disposing of a revision or appeal before it.
RatioOfTheDecision	The appellants, however, contend that, as the power of <ORG> to order transfer of cases has been specifically provided for in section 406 and would normally exclude cases of intra-state transfer covered by section 407 of the Code, the statute should not be so construed as to imply a power of <ORG>, in appeal or revision, to transfer a case from a subordinate court to <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The argument also is that what <ORG>, as an appellate or revisional court, could have done was either (a) to direct <ORG> to consider whether this was a fit case for it to exercise its power under section 407(1)(iv) to withdraw the case to itself and try the same with a view to expeditiously dispose it of or (b) to have withdrawn the case to itself for trial.
RatioOfTheDecision	But , it is contended, no power which <ORG> could exercise as an appellate or revisional court could have enabled <ORG> to transfer the case from the Special Judge to <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	16.
RatioOfTheDecision	Here also, the contentions of both parties are nicely balanced but I am inclined to think that had the matter been res integra and directions for transfer were being sought before us for the first time, this Court would have hesitated to issue such a direction and may at best have left it to <ORG> to consider the matter and exercise its own discretion.
RatioOfTheDecision	As already pointed out, the powers of <ORG> to transfer cases from one court to another are to be found in Article 139-A of the Constitution and section 406 of the .
RatioOfTheDecision	The provisions envisaged either inter-state transfers of cases i.e.
RatioOfTheDecision	from a court in one State to a court in another State or the withdrawal of a case by <ORG> to itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	Intra- State transfer among courts subordinate to <ORG> inter-se or from a court subo rdinate to <ORG> to <ORG> is within the jurisdiction of the appropriate <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The attempt of counsel for the resondent is to justify the transfer by attributing the powers of <ORG> under section 407 to <ORG> in its capacity as an appellate or revisional court.
RatioOfTheDecision	This argument overlooks that the powers of <ORG>, in disposing of an appeal or revision, are circumscribed by the scope of the proceedings before it.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this case, it is common ground that the question of transfer was not put in issue before <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	17.
RatioOfTheDecision	The reliance placed in this context on the provisions contained in articles 140 and 142 of the Constitution and S. 401 read with S. 386 of the .
RatioOfTheDecision	does not also help.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 140 is only a provisions enabling <ORG> to confer supplementary powers on <ORG> to enable it to deal more effectively to exercise the jurisdication conferred on it by or under the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 142 is also not of much assistance.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the first p lace, the operative words in that article, again are "in the exercise of its jurisdiction."
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> was hearing an appeal from the order of discharge and connected matters.
RatioOfTheDecision	There was no issue or controversy or discussion before it as to the comparative merits of a trial before a special judge vis-a-vis one before <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	There was only an oral request said to have been made, admittedly, after the judgment was announced.
RatioOfTheDecision	Wide as the powers under article 141 are, they do not in my view, envisage an order of the type presently in question.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Nanavati case (1961 SCR 497, to which reference was made by Sri <NAME>, involved a totally different type of situation.
RatioOfTheDecision	Secondly, it is one of the contentions of the appellant that an order of this type, far from being necessary for doing complete justice in the cause or matter pending before the <ORG>, has actually resulted in injustice, an aspect discussed a little later.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thirdly, however wide and plenary the language of the article, the directions  given by the <ORG> should not be inconsistent with, repugnant to or in violation of the specific provisions of any statute.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the provisions of the 1952 Act read with article 139-A and Ss.406-407 of the Cr.P.C .
RatioOfTheDecision	do not permit the transfer of the case from a special judge to the High <ORG>, that effect cannot be achieved indirectly.
RatioOfTheDecision	it is, therefore, difficult to say, in the circumstances of the case, that the Supreme <ORG> can issue the impugned direction in exercise of the powers under Article 142 or under s. 407 available to it as an appellate court.
RatioOfTheDecision	18.
Argument	Learned counsel for the complainant also sought to support the order of transfer by reference to section 386 and 401 of the 1973 Cr.P.C .
Argument	He suggested that the <ORG>, having set aside the order of discharge, had necessarily to think about consequential orders and that such directions as were issued are fully justified by the above provisions.
Argument	He relied in this context on the decision of <ORG> in <CITATION_1314305> , AIR 1935 P.C.122.
Argument	It is dif ficult to accept this argument.
Argument	Section 401 provides that, in the revision pending before it, <ORG> can exercise any of the powers conferred on a court of appeal under section 386 .
Precedent	Section 386 , dealing with the powers of the appellate court enables the court, in a case such as this: (i) under clause (a), to alter or reverse the order under appeal/revision; or (ii) under clause (e), to make any amendment or any consequential or incidental order that may be just or proper.
Precedent	The decision relied on by counsel, <CITATION_1314305> , AIR 1935 P.C.
Precedent	122, is of no assistance to him.
Precedent	In that case, the Additional Judicial Commissioner, who heard an appeal on a difference of opinion between two other judicial commissioner had come to the conclusion that the conviction had to be set aside.
Precedent	Then he had the duty to determine what should be done and S. 426 of the 1898 Cr.P.C .
RatioOfTheDecision	(corresponding to section 386 of the 1973 Cr.P.C .)
RatioOfTheDecision	exactly provided for the situation and empowered him:  "to reverse the finding and senten ce and acquit or discharge the accused or order him to be retried by a court of competent jurisdiction subordinate to such apellate <ORG>."
RatioOfTheDecision	In the present case, the Special Judge.
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME>, had discharged the accused because of his conclusion, that the prosecution lacked the necessary sanction.
RatioOfTheDecision	The conclusion of <ORG> that this conclusion was wrong meant, automatically, that the prosecution had been properly initiated and that the proceedings before the Special Judge should go on.
RatioOfTheDecision	The direction that the trial should be shifted to <ORG> can hardly be described as a consequential or incidental order.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such a direction did not flow, as a necessary consequence of the conclusion of the court on the issues and points debated before it.
RatioOfTheDecision	I am, therefore, inclined to agree with counsel for the appellant that this <ORG> was in error when it directed that the trial of the case should be before a High <ORG> Judge.
RatioOfTheDecision	19.
Argument	It follows from the above discussion that the appellant, in consequence of the imp ugned direction, is being tried by a '<ORG> which has no jurisdiction-and which cannot be empowered by the Supreme <ORG>-to try him.
Argument	The continued trial before the High <ORG>, therefore, infringes Article 21 of the Constitution.
Argument	Denial of equality and violation of Article 21.
Argument	20.
Argument	It was vehemently contended for the appellant that, by giving the impugned direction, this <ORG> has deprived the appellant of his fundamental rights.
Argument	He has been denied a right to equality, inasmuch as his case has been singled out for trial by a different, though higher, forum as compared to other public servants.
Argument	His fundamental right under Article 21 , it is said, has been violated, inasmuch as the direction has deprived him of a right of revision and first appeal to the High <ORG> which he would have had from an order or sentence had he been tried by a Special Judge and it is doubtful whether he would have a right to appeal to this <ORG> at all.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is pointed out that a right of first appeal against a conviction in a criminal  case has been held, by this <ORG>, to be a part of the fundamental right guaranteed under Article 21 of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is not necessary for me to consider these arguments in view of my conclusion that the High <ORG> could not have been directed to try the petitioner's case.
RatioOfTheDecision	I would, however, like to say that, in my opinion, the arguments based on Articles 14 and 21 cannot be accepted, in case it is to be held for any reason that the transfer of the apellant's case to the High <ORG> was valid and within the competence of this <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	I say this for the following reason: If the argument is to be accepted, it will be appreciated, it cannot be confined to cases of transfer to the High <ORG> of cases under the 1952 Act but would also be equally valid to impugn the withdrawal of a criminal case tried in the normal course under the Cr.P.C .
RatioOfTheDecision	from a subordinate court trying it to the High <ORG> by invoking the powers under section 407 .
RatioOfTheDecision	To put it in other words, the argument, in substance, assails the validity o f secion 407 of the 1973 Cr.P.C .
RatioOfTheDecision	In my opinion, this attack has to be repelled.
RatioOfTheDecision	The section cannot be challenged under Article 14 as it is based on a reasonable classification having relation to the objects sought to be achieved.
RatioOfTheDecision	Though, in general, the trial of cases will be by courts having the normal jurisdiction over them, the exigencies of the situation may require that they be dealt with by some other court for various reasons.
RatioOfTheDecision	Likewise, the nature of a case, the nature of issues involved and other circumstances may render it more expedient, effective, expeditious or desirable that the case should be tried by a superior court or <ORG> itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	The power of transfer and withdrawal contained in s. 407 of the .
Precedent	is one dictated by the requirements of justice and is, indeed, but an aspect of the supervisory powers of a superior court over courts subordinate to it: (see also sections 408 to 411 of the .
Precedent	).
Precedent	A judicial discretion to transfer or withdraw is vested in the highest court of the <ORG> and is  made exercisable only in the circumstances set out in the section.
Precedent	Such a power is not only necessary and desirable but indispensable in the cause of the administration of justice.
Precedent	The accused will continue to be tried by a court of equal or superior jurisdiction.
Precedent	Section 407(8) read with S. 474 of the .
Precedent	and section 8(3) of the 1952 Act makes it clear that he will be tried in accordance with the procedure followed by the original <ORG> or ordinarily by a <ORG> of Session.
Precedent	The accused will, therefore, suffer no prejudice by reason of the application of s. 407 .
Precedent	Even if there is a differential treatment which causes prejudice, it is based on logical and acceptable considerations with a view to promote the interest of justice.
Precedent	The transfer or withdrawal of a case to another court or the High <ORG>, in such circumstances, can hardly be said to result in hostile discrimination against the accused in such a case.
Precedent	21.
Precedent	Considerable reliance was placed on behalf of the appellant on <CITATION_184660633> ,  S.C.R.
Precedent	284 .
Precedent	This decision seems to have influenced the learned judges before whom this appeal first came up for hearing in referring the matter to this larger Bench and has also been aplied to the facts and situation here by my learned brother, <NAME>, J.
RatioOfTheDecision	But it seems to me that the said decision has no relevance here.
RatioOfTheDecision	There, the category of cases which were to be allocated to a Special Judge were not well defined; the selection of cases was to be made by the executive; and the procedure to be followed by the special courts was different from the normal criminal procedure.
RatioOfTheDecision	As already pointed out, the position here is entirely different.
RatioOfTheDecision	The 1952 legislation has been enacted to give effect to <ORG> and to remedy a state of affairs prevalent in respect of a well defined class of offences and its provisions constituting special judges to try offences of corruption is not under challenge.
RatioOfTheDecision	Only a power of transfer is being exercised by <ORG> which is sought to be traced back to the  power of <ORG> under s. 407 .
RatioOfTheDecision	The vires of that provision also is not being challenged.
RatioOfTheDecision	What is perhaps being said is that <ORG> ought not to have considered this case a fit one for withdrawal for trial to <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	That plea should be and is being considered here on merits but the plea that Article 14 has been violated by the exercise of a power under s. 407 on the strength of <NAME> case wholly appears to be untenable.
RatioOfTheDecision	Reference may be made in this context to <CITATION_1949862> ,  3 S.C.R.
Fact	435 and Re: Special Courts Bill, 1978,  2 S.C.R.
Precedent	476 and <CITATION_220229> ,  3 S.C.R.
Precedent	500, which have upheld the creation of special judges to try certain classes of offences.
Precedent	22.
RatioOfTheDecision	It may be convenient at this place to refer to certain observations by the <ORG> of this Court, while referring this matter to the larger <ORG>, in a note appended to their order on this aspect.
RatioOfTheDecision	The learned Judges have posed the following questions in paragraphs 4 and 6 of their note:  138  "4.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Crimi nal Law Amendment Act , 1952 as its preamble says is passed to provide for speedier trial?
RatioOfTheDecision	Does not further speeding up of the case by transferring the case to <ORG> for speedy disposal violate the principle laid down by seven learned Judges of this Court in <NAME> case (1952) S.C.R.
Argument	284 and result in violation of Article 14 of the Constitution?
Precedent	The following observations of <NAME>, J. in <NAME> case at pages 366-387 of the Report are relevant:  'Tested in the light of these considerations, I am of opinion that the whole of the West Bengal Special Courts Act of 1950 offends the provisions of Article 14 and is therefore bad.
Precedent	When the froth and the foam of discussion is cleared away and learned dialectics placed on one side, we reach at last the human element which to my mind is the most important of all.
Precedent	We find men accused of heinous crimes called upon to answer for their lives and liberties.
Precedent	We find them picked out from their fellows, and however much the new procedure m ay give them a few crumbs of advantage, in the bulk they are deprived of substantial and valuable privileges of defence which others, similarly charged, are able to claim.
Precedent	It matters not to me, nor indeed to them and their families and their friends, whether this be done in good faith, whether it be done for the convenience of government, whether the process can be scientifically classified and labelled, or whether it is an experiment in speedier trials made for the good of society at large.
RatioOfTheDecision	It matters now how lofty and laudable the motives are.
RatioOfTheDecision	The question with which I charge myself is, can fair-minded, reasonable, unbiassed and resolute men, who are not swayed by emotion or prejudice, regard this with equanimity and call it reasonable, just and fair, regard it as that equal treatment and protection in the defence of liberties which is expected of a sovereign democratic republic in the conditions which obtain in India today?
RatioOfTheDecision	I have but one answer to that.
Precedent	On that short and simple ground I would decide this  case and hold the Act bad.'
Precedent	(Underlining by us) Do not the above observations apply to judicial orders also?
Precedent	139  6.
Precedent	Does the degree of heinousness of the crime with which an accused is charged or his status or the influence that he commands in society have any bearing on the applicability or the constriction of Article 14 or Article 21.?"
Precedent	23.
RatioOfTheDecision	In my opinion, the answers to the questions posed will, again, depend on whether the impugned direction can be brought within the scope of section 407 of the 1973 Cr.P.C .
RatioOfTheDecision	Or not.
RatioOfTheDecision	If I am right in my conclusion that it cannot, the direction will clearly be contrary to the provisions of the Cr.P.C .
RatioOfTheDecision	and hence violative of Article 21.
RatioOfTheDecision	It could also perhaps be said to be discriminatory on the ground that, in the absence of not only a statutory provision but even any well defined policy or criteria, the only two reasons given in the order-namely, the status of the petitioner and delay in the progress of the trial-are inadequate to justify the special treatment meted out t o the appellant.
RatioOfTheDecision	On the other hand, if the provisions of section 407 .
RatioOfTheDecision	are applicable, the direction will be in consonance with a procedure prescribed by law and hence safe from attack as violative of Article 21.
RatioOfTheDecision	The reasons given, in the context of the developments in the case, can also be sought to be justified in terms of clauses (a), (b) or (c) of Section 407(1) .
RatioOfTheDecision	In such an event, the direction will not amount to an arbitrary discrimination but can be justified as the exercise of a choice of courses permitted under a valid statutory classification intended to serve a public purpose.
RatioOfTheDecision	24.
RatioOfTheDecision	The argument of infringment of article 21 is based essentially on the premise that the accused will be deprived, in cases where the trial is withdrawn to <ORG> of a right of first appeal.
RatioOfTheDecision	This fear is entirely unfounded.
RatioOfTheDecision	I think Sri <NAME> is right in contending that where a case is thus withdrawn and tried by the <ORG>, <ORG> will be conducting the trial in the exercise of its extraordinary origina l criminal jurisdiction.
RatioOfTheDecision	As pointed out by <NAME>, J., the old Presidency- town <ORG> once exercised original jurisdiction in criminal matters but this has since been abolished.
RatioOfTheDecision	One possible view is that now all original criminal jurisdiction exercised by <ORG> is only extraordinary original criminal jurisdiction.
RatioOfTheDecision	Another possible view is that still <ORG> do exercise ordinary original criminal jurisdiction in habeas corpus and contempt of court matters and also under some specific enactments (e.g.
RatioOfTheDecision	Companies' Act Ss.
RatioOfTheDecision	454 and 633).
RatioOfTheDecision	They can be properly described as exercising extraordinary original criminal jurisdiction, where though the ordinary original criminal jurisdiction is vested in a subordinate criminal court or special Judge, a case is withdrawn by the <ORG> to itself for trial.
Precedent	The decision in Madura Tirupparankundram etc.
Precedent	v. <NAME>, 35 C.W.N.
Precedent	1088, <CITATION_822294> , AIR 1949 Bombay 42, <CITATION_546089> , AIR 1954 Calcutta 305, <ORG> <CITATION_1454493> ,  AIR 1961 Punjab 87 and People's <CITATION_1239298> ,  Crl.
Precedent	L.J .
RatioOfTheDecision	545 cited by him amply support this contention.
RatioOfTheDecision	If this be so, then Sri <NAME> is also right in saying that a right of first appeal to <ORG> against the order passed by <ORG> will be available to the accused under s. 374 of the 1973 Cr.P.C .
RatioOfTheDecision	In other words, in the ordinary run of criminal cases tried by <ORG>, the accused will be tried in the first instance by a court subordinate to <ORG>; he will then have a right of first appeal to <ORG> and then can seek leave of <ORG> to appeal to it under Article 136.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the case of a withdrawn case, the accused has the privilege of being tried in the first instance by <ORG> itself with a right to approach the apex Court by way of appeal.
RatioOfTheDecision	The apprehension that the judgment in the trial by <ORG>, in the latter case, will be final, with only a chance of obtaining special leave under article 136 is totally unfounded.
RatioOfTheDecision	There  is also some force in the submission of Sri <NAME> that, if that really be the position and the appellant had no right of appeal against <ORG> judgment, <ORG> will consider any petition presented under Article 136 in the light of the inbuilt requirements of Article 21 and dispose of it as if it were itself a petition of appeal from the judgment.
Precedent	(see, in this context, the observations of this <ORG> in <NAME> v. <NAME>,  2 S.C.R.
Precedent	673.
RatioOfTheDecision	That, apart it may be pointed out, this is also an argument that would be valid in respect even of ordinary criminal trials withdrawn to the High <ORG> under s. 407 of the .
RatioOfTheDecision	and thus, like the previous argument regarding Article 14 , indirectly challenges the validity of S.407 itself as infringing Article 21.
RatioOfTheDecision	For the reasons discussed, I have come to the conclusion that an accused, tried directly by the High <ORG> by withdrawal of his case from a subordinate court, has a right of appeal to <ORG> under s. 374 of the .
RatioOfTheDecision	The allegation  of an infringement of Article 2 1 in such cases is.
Precedent	therefore.
Precedent	unfounded.
Precedent	Natural Justice  25.
RatioOfTheDecision	The appellant's contention that the impugned direction is sued by this <ORG> on 16.2.1984 was in violation of the principles of natural justice appears to be well founded.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is really not in dispute before us that there was no whisper or suggestion in the proceedings before this <ORG> that the venue of the trial should be shifted to <ORG>.
Argument	This direction was issued suo motu by the learned Judges without putting it to the counsel for the parties that this was what they proposed to do.
Argument	The difficulties created by observations or directions on issues not debated before the <ORG> have been highlighted by Lord <NAME> in <ORG> v. <NAME>, [1983] A.C. 191).
Argument	In that case, Lord <NAME>, in the <ORG> of Appeal, had in his judgment, relied on a certain passage from the speech of Lord <NAME> in <ORG> as reported in Hansard (<ORG>ary Reports) in support of the view taken by him.
Argument	The c ounsel for the parties had had no inkling or information that recourse was likely to be had by the Judge to this source, as it had been authoritatively held by <ORG> in <NAME> v. <NAME>, [1979] A.C. 264 that these reports should not be referred to by counsel or relied upon by the court for any purpose.
Argument	Commenting on this aspect, Lord <NAME> observed:  "Under our adversary system of procedure, for a judge to disregard the rule by which counsel are bound has the effect of depriving the parties to the action of the benefit of one of the most fundamental rules of natural justice: the right of each to be informed of any point adverse to him that is going to be relied upon by the judge and to be given an opportunity of stating what his answer to it is.
Argument	In the instant case, counsel for <NAME> and <NAME> complained that Lord <NAME> had selected one speech alone to rely upon out of many that had been made .. and that, if he has counsel had known that (Lord <NAME>) was going to do that, not only wou ld he have wished to criticise what Lord <NAME> had said in his speech ....... but he would also have wished to rely on other speeches disagreeing with Lord <NAME> if he, as counsel, had been entitled to refer to <NAME> ....."  The position is somewhat worse in the present case.
Precedent	Unlike the <NAME> case (supra) where the Judge had only used <NAME> to deal with an issue that arose in the appeal, the direction in the present case was something totally alien to the scope of the appeal, on an issue that was neither raised nor debated in the course of the hearing and completely unexpected.
Precedent	26.
Argument	<NAME> submitted that, when the judgment was announced, counsel for the complainant (present respondent) had made an oral request that the trial be transferred to <ORG> and that the Judges replied that they had already done that.
Argument	He submitted that, at that time and subsequently, the appellant could have protested and put forward his objections but did not and had thus acquiesced in a direction wh ich was, in truth, beneficial to him as this <ORG> had only directed that he should be tried by a High <ORG> Judge, a direction against which no one can reasonably complain.
RatioOfTheDecision	This aspect of the respondent's arguments will be dealt with later but, for the present, all that is necessary is to say that the direction must have come as a surprise to the appellant and had been issued without hearing him on the course proposed to be adopted.
RatioOfTheDecision	Conclusion  27.
RatioOfTheDecision	To sum up, my conclusion on issue A is that the direction issued by the <ORG> was not warranted in law, being contrary to the special provisions of the 1952 Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	was also not in conformity with the principles of natural justice and that, unless the direction can be justified with reference to S. 407 of the Cr.
RatioOfTheDecision	P.C., the petitioner's fundamental rights under Articles 14 and 21 can be said to have been infringed by reason of this direction.
RatioOfTheDecision	This takes me on to the question whether it follows as a consequence that the direction issued can be, or should be, recalled,  annulled, revoked or set aside by us now.
RatioOfTheDecision	B.
Precedent	CAN AND SHOULD THE DIRECTION OF 16.2.84 BE RECALLED?
Precedent	28.
Precedent	It will be appreciated that, whatever may be the ultimate conclusion on the correctness, propriety or otherwise of the <ORG>'s direction dated 16.2.1984, that was a direction given by this <ORG> in a proceeding between the same parties and the important and farreaching question that falls for consideration is whether it is at all open to the appellant to seek to challenge the correctness of that direction at a later stage of the same trial.
Precedent	Is a review possible?
Precedent	29.
Precedent	The first thought that would occur to any one who seeks a modification of an order of this <ORG>, particularly on the ground that it contained a direction regarding which he had not been heard, would be to seek a review of that order under Article 137 of the Constitution read with the relevant rules.
Precedent	Realising that this would be a direct and straight forward remedy, it was contended for the appellant that the present appeal may be treated as  an application for such review.
Precedent	30.
Precedent	The power of review is conferred on this <ORG> by Article 137 of the Constitution which reads thus:  143  "Subject to the provisions of any law made by <ORG> or any rules made under Article 145 , the Supreme <ORG> shall have power to review any judgment pronounced or order made by it."
RatioOfTheDecision	It is subject not only to the provisions of any law made by <ORG> (and there is no such law so far framed) but also to any rules made by this <ORG> under Article 145.
RatioOfTheDecision	This <ORG> has made rules in pursuance of art.
RatioOfTheDecision	145 which are contained in order XL in Part VIII of the Supreme <ORG> Rules.
RatioOfTheDecision	Three of these rules are relevant for our present purposes.
Precedent	They read as follows:  "(1) The <ORG> may review its judgment or order, but no appliction for review will be entertained in a civil proceeding except on the ground mentioned in order XLVII, rule 1 of the Code , and in a criminal proceeding except on the ground of an error apparent on the face of the record.
Precedent	(Z) An application for review  shall be by a petition.
Precedent	and shall be filed within thirty days from the date of the judgment or order sought to be reviewed.
Precedent	It shall set out clearly its grounds for review.
Precedent	(3) Unless otherwise ordered by the <ORG> an application for review shall be disposed of by circulation without any oral arguments, but the petitioner may supplement his petition by additional written arguments.
Precedent	The court may either dismiss the petition or direct notice to the opposite party.
Precedent	An application for review shall as far as practicable be circulated to the same Judge or Bench of Judges that delivered the judgment or order sought to be reviewed."
Precedent	31.
Precedent	It is contended on behalf of the respondent that the present pleas of the appellant cannot be treated as an application for review, firstly, because they do not seek to rectify any error apparent on the face of the record; secondly, because the prayer is being made after the expiry of the period of thirty days mentioned in rule 2 and there is no sufficient cause for condoning the d elay in the making of the application and thirdly, for the reason that a review petition has to be listed as far as practicable before the same Judge or Bench of Judges that delivered the order sought to be reviewed and in this case at least two of the learned Judges, who passed the order on 16.2.1984, are still available to consider the application for review.
Precedent	These grounds may now be considered.
Precedent	144  32.
RatioOfTheDecision	For reasons which I shall later discuss, I am of opinion that the order dated 16.2.1984 does not suffer from any error apparent on the face of the record which can be rectified on a review application.
RatioOfTheDecision	So far as the second point is concerned, it is common ground that the prayer for review has been made beyond the period mentioned in Rule 2 of order XL of the Supreme <ORG> Rules.
RatioOfTheDecision	No doubt this <ORG> has power to extend the time within which a review petition may be filed but learned counsel for the respondent vehemently contended that this is not a fit case for exercising the power of condonation of delay.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is urged that, far from this being a fit case for the entertainment of the application for review beyond the time prescribed, the history of the case will show that the petitioner has deliberately avoided filing a review petition within the time prescribed for reasons best known to himself .
RatioOfTheDecision	33.
RatioOfTheDecision	In support of his contention, learned counsel for the respondent invited our attention to the following sequence of events and made the following points:  (a) The order of this <ORG> was passed on 16.2.1984.
RatioOfTheDecision	At the time of the pronouncement of the said order, counsel for the present respondent had made a request that the trial of the case may be shifted to <ORG> and the <ORG> had observed that a direction to this effect had been included in the judgment.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even assuming that there had been no issues raised and no arguments advanced on the question of transfer at the time of the hearing of the appeals, there was nothing to preclude the counsel for the appellant, when the counsel for the complainant made the  above request, from contending that it should not be done, or, at least, that it should not be done without further hearing him and pointing out this was not a matter which had been debated at the hearing of the appeal.
RatioOfTheDecision	But no, the counsel for the accused chose to remain quiet and did not raise any objection at that point of time.
RatioOfTheDecision	He could have filed a review application soon thereafter but he did not do so.
RatioOfTheDecision	Perhaps he considered, at that stage, that the order which after all enabled him to be tried by a <ORG> Judge in preference to a Special Judge was favourable to him and, therefore, he did not choose to object.
RatioOfTheDecision	(b) The matter came up before the trial judge on 13th March, 1984.
Argument	The accused, who appeared in person, stated that he did not want to engage any counsel "at least for the present'.
Argument	A He would not put down his arguments in writing and when he argued the gravemen of his attack was that this <ORG>'s order transferring the trial from the Special Judge to the <ORG> was wrong on merits.
RatioOfTheDecision	Natura lly, the learned Judge found it difficult to accept the contention that he should go behind the order of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	He rightly pointed out that if the accused had any grievance to make, his proper remedy was to move <ORG> for review of its judgment or for such further directions or clarifications as may be expedient.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus, as early as 13th March, 1984, <NAME>, J., had given a specific opportunity to the accused to come to this <ORG> and seek a review of the direction.
RatioOfTheDecision	it can perhaps be said that on 16.2.1984, when this <ORG> passed the impugned direction, the appellant was not fully conscious of the impact of the said direction and that, therefore, he did not object to it immediately.
RatioOfTheDecision	But, by the 13th March, 1984, he had ample time to think about the matter and to consult his counsel.
RatioOfTheDecision	The appellant himself was a barrister.
RatioOfTheDecision	He chose not to engage counsel but to argue himself and, even after the trial court specifically pointed out to him that it was bound by the direction of this <ORG> unde r Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	141 and 144 of the Constitution and that, if at all, his remedy was to go to <ORG> by way of review or by way of an application for clarification, he chose to take no action thereon.
RatioOfTheDecision	c) on 16th March, 1984, <NAME>, J. disposed of the preliminary objections raised by the accused challenging the jurisdiction and competence of this <ORG> to try the accused.
Argument	Counsel for the respondent points out that, at the time of the hearing, the appellant had urged before <NAME>, J. all the objections to the trial, which he is now putting forth.
Argument	These objections have been summarised in paragraph 3 of the order passed by the learned Judge and each one of them has been dealt with elaborately by the learned Judge.
Argument	It has been pointed out by him that <ORG> was considering not only the appeals preferred by the accused and the complainant, namely, Crl.
Argument	Appeal Nos.
Precedent	246, 247 and 356 of 1983 but also two revision petitions being C.R.
Precedent	Nos.
Precedent	354 'and 359 of 1983 which had been withdrawn by <ORG> rt to itself for disposal along with Crl.
Precedent	Appeal No.
Precedent	356 of 1983.
Precedent	A little later in the order the learned Judge pointed out that, even assuming that in the first instance the trial can be conducted only by a Special Judge, the proceedings could be withdrawn by the high Court to itself under powers vested in it under Article 228(a) of the Constitution as well as section 407 of the .
Precedent	When the criminal revisions stood transferred to <ORG> (this was obviously done under Article 139-A though that article is not specifically mentioned in the judgment of <ORG>), <ORG> could pass the order under Article 139-A read with Article 142.
Precedent	The learned Judge also disposed of the objections based on Article 21.
Precedent	He pointed out that as against an ordinary accused person tried by a special judge, who gets a right of appeal to <ORG>, a court of superior jurisdiction, with a further right of appeal to <ORG> under s. 374 of the .
RatioOfTheDecision	and that an order of transfer passed in the int erest of expeditious disposal of a trial was primarily in the interests of the accused and could hardly be said to be pre judicial to the accused.
RatioOfTheDecision	Despite the very careful and fully detailed reasons passed by <ORG>, the appellant did not choose to seek a review of the earlier direction.
RatioOfTheDecision	(d) Against the order of the learned Judge dated 16.3.1984 the complainant came to the Court because he was dissatisfied with certain observations made by the trial Judge in regard to the procedure to be followed by <ORG> in proceeding with the trial.
Fact	This matter was heard in open court by same five learned Judges who had disposed of the matter earlier on 16.2.1984.
Fact	The accused was represented by a senior counsel and <ORG> had also engaged a senior counsel to represent its case.
Fact	Even at this hearing the counsel for the appellant did not choose to raise any objection against the direction given in the order dated 16.2.1984.
Fact	The appeal before <ORG> was for getting a clarific ation of the very order dated 16.2.1984.
Argument	This was a golden opportunity for the appellant also to seek a review or clarification of the impugned direction, if really he had a grievance that he had not been heard by the <ORG> before it issued the direction and that it was also contrary to the provisions of the 1952 Act as well as violative of the rights of the accused under Art.
Argument	21 of the Constitution.
Argument	(e) The petitioner instead filed two special leave petitions and a writ petition against the orders of <NAME> dated 13.3.1984 and 16.3.1984.
Argument	In the writ petition, the petitioner had mentioned that the impugned direction had been issued without hearing him.
Argument	In these matters counsel for the accused made both oral and written submissions and all contentions and arguments, which have now been put forward, had been raised in the written arguments.
Argument	The appeals and writ petition were disposed of by this <ORG>.
Argument	This <ORG> naturally dismissed the special leave petitions pointing out that the High <ORG> was quite corr ect in considering itself bound by the directions of the <ORG>.
Argument	The <ORG> also dismissed the writ petition as without merit.
Argument	But once again it observed that the proper remedy of the petitioner was elsewhere and not by way of a writ petition.
Argument	These two orders, according to the learned counsel for the respondent, conclude the matter against the appellant.
Argument	The dismissal of the writ petition reminded the petitioner of his right to move the <ORG> by other means and, though this advice was tendered as early as 17.4.1984, the petitioner did nothing.
Argument	So far as the special leave petition was concerned, its dismissal meant the affirmation in full of the decision given by Justice <NAME> dismissing and disposing of all the objections raised by the petitioner before him.
Argument	Whatever may have been the position on 16.2.1984 or 16.3.1984, there was absolutely no explanation or justification for the conduct of the petitioner in failing to file an application for review between 17.4.1984 and october, 1986.
Argument	34.
Argument	Recounting the abo ve history, which according to him fully explained the attitude of the accused, learned counsel for the respondent submitted that in his view the appellant was obviously trying to avoid a review petition perhaps because it was likely to go before the same learned Judges and he did not think that he would get any relief and perhaps also because he might have felt that a review was not an adequate remedy for him as, under the rules, it would be disposed of in chamber without hearing him once again.
RatioOfTheDecision	But, whatever may be the reason, it is submitted, the delay between April 1984 and october, 1986 stood totally unexplained and even now there was no proper review petition before this <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the circumstances, it is urged that this present belated prayer for review.
RatioOfTheDecision	35.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is substance in these contentions.
RatioOfTheDecision	The prayer for review is being made very belatedly, and having regard to the circumstances outlined above there is hardly any reason to condone the delay in the prayer for review.
RatioOfTheDecision	The appellant was alive to  all his present contentions as is seen from the papers in the writ petition.
RatioOfTheDecision	At least when the writ petition was dismissed as an inappropriate remedy, he should have at once moved this <ORG> for review.
RatioOfTheDecision	The delay from April 1984 to october 1986 is totally inexplicable.
RatioOfTheDecision	That apart, there is also validity in the respondent's contention that.
RatioOfTheDecision	even if we are inclined to condone the delay, the application will have to be heard as far as possible by the same learned Judges who disposed of the earlier matter.
RatioOfTheDecision	In other words, that application will have to be heard by a Bench which includes the two learned Judges who disposed of the appeal on 16.2.1984 and who are still available in this <ORG> to deal with any proper review application, that may be filed.
Precedent	However, since in my view, the delay has not been satisfactorily explained, I am of opinion that the prayer of the appellant that the present pleas may be treated as one in the nature of a review application and the appellant given relief on that basis has to be rej ected.
Precedent	Is a writ maintainable?
Precedent	36.
Precedent	This takes one to a consideration of the second line of attack by the appellant's counsel.
Precedent	His proposition was that a judicial order of a court-even the <ORG> or this <ORG> may breach the principles of natural justice or the fundamental rights and that, if it does so, it can be quashed by this <ORG> in the exercise of its jurisdiction under Article 32.
Precedent	In other words, the plea would seem to be that the present proceedings may be treated as in the nature of a writ petition to quash the impugned order on the above ground.
Precedent	The earliest of the cases relied upon to support this contention is the decision in <CITATION_1945293> ,  Supp.
Precedent	1 S.C.R.
Precedent	885, which may perhaps be described as the sheet-anchor of the appellant's contentions on this point.
Fact	The facts of that case have been set out in the judgment of <NAME> J. and need not be repeated.
Fact	The case was heard by a Bench of five judges.
Argument	Four of them, speaking through <NAME>, J. held that Rule 12 of order X XXV of the Supreme <ORG> Rules violated Article 32 and declared it invalid.
Argument	This also set aside an earlier order dated 12.12.1961 passed by the <ORG> in pursuance of the rule calling upon the petitioner to deposit cash security.
Argument	<NAME> contended that this case involved two separate issues for consideration by the <ORG>:  (a) the validity of the rule and (b) the validity of the order dated 12.12.1961; and that the decision is authority not only for the proposition that a writ petition under Article 32 could be filed to Impugn the constitutional validity of a rule but also for the proposition that the <ORG> could entertain a writ petition to set aside a judicial Order passed by the <ORG> earlier on discovering that it is inconsistent with the fundamental rights of the petitioner.
Argument	Counsel submitted that an impression in the minds of some persons that the decision in <ORG> is not good law after the decision of the nine-Judge Bench in <CITATION_1643138> , 3 S.C.R.
Precedent	744 is incorrect.
Precedent	He submitted that, fa r from <NAME>'s case being overruled, it has been confirmed in the later case.
Precedent	37.
Precedent	<NAME> was a case in which the validity of an interlocutory order passed by a judge of <ORG> pertaining to the publication of reports of the proceedings in a suit pending before him was challenged by a journalist as violating his fundamental rights under Article 19 of the Constitution.
Precedent	The matter came to <ORG> by way of a writ petition under Article 32.
Precedent	The validity of the order was upheld by the majority of the Judges while <NAME> dissented.
Precedent	In this connection it is necessary to refer to a passage at p. 767 in the judgment of <NAME>, C.J.
Precedent	"Mr. <NAME> has conceded that if a court of competent jurisdiction makes an order in a proceeding before it, and the order is inter- partes, its validity cannot be challenged by invoking the jurisdiction of this <ORG> under Art.
Precedent	32 , though the said order may affect the aggrieved party's fundamental rights.
Precedent	His whole argument before us has been  that the impugned order affects the fundamental rights of a stranger to the proceeding before the <ORG>; and that, he contends, justifies the petitioners in moving this <ORG> under Artc.
Precedent	32.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is necessary to examine the validity of this argument.
RatioOfTheDecision	The question before the Supreme <ORG> was thus as to whether, even at the instance of a stranger to the earlier proceedings, the earlier order could be challenged by means of a writ petition under Article 32.
RatioOfTheDecision	One of the questions that had to be considered by the <ORG> was whether the judicial order passed by the learned judge of the High <ORG> was amenable to be writ jurisdiction of the <ORG> under Article 32.
Precedent	On this question, the judges reacted differently:  (i) <NAME>, CJ and <NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME> and <NAME>, JJ.
Precedent	had this to say:  "The High <ORG> is a superior <ORG> of Record and it is for it to consider whether any matter falls within its jurisdiction Or not.
Precedent	The order is a judicial order and if it is erroneous, a person aggrieved by it, though  a stranger, could move this <ORG> under Article 136 and the order can be corrected in appeal but the question about the existence of the said jurisdiction as well as the validity or the propriety of the order cannot be raised in writ proceedings under article 32.
Precedent	',  (ii) <NAME> also concurred in the view that this <ORG> had no power to issue a certiorari to the High <ORG>.
Precedent	He observed:  "I confess the question is of some haziness.
Precedent	That haziness arises because the courts in our country which have been given the power to issue the writ are not fully analogous to the English courts having that power.
Precedent	We have to seek a way out for ourselves.
Precedent	Having given the matter my best consideration, I venture to think that it was not contemplated that a High <ORG> is an inferior court even though it is a court of limited jurisdiction.
Precedent	The Constitution gave power to the High <ORG> to issue the writ.
Precedent	In England, an inferior court could never issue the writ.
RatioOfTheDecision	I think it would be abhorrent to the principle of certiorari if  a <ORG> which can itself issue the writ is to be made subject to be corrected by a writ issued by another court.
RatioOfTheDecision	When a court has the power to issue the writ, it is not according to the fundamental principles of certiorari, an inferior court or a court of limited jurisdiction.
RatioOfTheDecision	It does not cease to be so because another <ORG> to which appeals from it lie has also the power to issue the writ.
RatioOfTheDecision	That should furnish strong justification for saying that the Constitution did not contemplate the High <ORG>s to be inferior courts so that their decisions would be liable to be quashed by writs issued by the Supreme <ORG> which also had been given power to issue the writs.
RatioOfTheDecision	Nor do I think that the cause of justice will in any manner be affected if a High <ORG> is not made amenable to correct by this <ORG> by the issue of the writ.
Precedent	In my opinion, therefore, this <ORG> has not power to issue a certiorari to a High <ORG>."
Precedent	(iii) <NAME> held:  "The High <ORG> has jurisdiction to decide if it could restrain the publicatio n of any document or information relating to the trial of a pending suit or concerning which the suit is brought, if it erroneously assume a jurisdiction not vested in it, its decision may be set aside in appropriate proceedings but the decision is not open to attack under Article 32 on the ground that it infringes the fundamental right under Article 19(1)(a).
Precedent	If a stranger is prejudiced by an order forbidding the publication of the report of any proceeding, his proper course is only to apply to the <ORG> tn lift the ban "  (iv) Justice <NAME> thought that, in principle, a writ petition could perhaps be filed to challenge an order of a High <ORG> on the ground that it violated the fundamental rights of the petitioner under Articles 20, 21 and 22 but he left the question open.
Precedent	He, however, concluded that an order of the nature in issue before the <ORG> could not be said to infringe Article 19.
Precedent	38.
Precedent	<NAME>, as His Lordship then was, however, dissented.
Precedent	He observed:  "Even assuming the impugned order mea ns a temporary suppression of the evidence of the witness, the trial Judge had no jurisdiction to pass the order.
Precedent	As he passed no recorded order, the appropriate remedy (in fact the only effective remedy) is to seek to quash the order by a writ under Article 32.
Precedent	There may be action by a Judge which may offend the fundamental rights under articles 14, 15, 19, 20, 21 and 22 and an appeal to this <ORG> will not only be practicable but will also be an ineffective remedy and this <ORG> can issue a writ to the High <ORG> to quash its order under Article 32 of the Constitution.
Precedent	Since there is no exception in Article 32 in respect of the High <ORG>s there is a presumption that the High <ORG>s are not excluded.
Precedent	Even with the enactment of Article 226 , the power which is conferred on the High <ORG> is not in every sense a coordinate power and the implication of reading articles 32, 136 and 226 together is that there is no sharing of the powers to issue the prerogative writs possessed by this <ORG>.
Precedent	Under the total sc heme of the Constitution, the subordination of <ORG> to <ORG> is not only evident but is logical."
Precedent	His Lordship proceeded to meet an objection that such a course might cast a slur on <ORG> or open the floodgates of litigation.
Precedent	He observed:  " Article 32 is concerned with Fundamental Rights and Fundamental Rights only.
Precedent	It is not concerned with breaches of law which do not involve fundamental rights directly.
Precedent	The ordinary writs of certiorari, mandamus and prohibition can only issue by enforcement of Fundamental Rights.
Precedent	A clear cut case of breach of Fundamental Right alone can be the basis for the exercise of this power.
Precedent	I have already given examples of actions of courts and judges which are not instances of wrong judicial orders capable of being brought before this court only by appeal but breaches of Fundamental Rights clear and simple.
Precedent	Denial of equality as for example by excluding members of a particular party or of a particular community from the public court room in a publi c hearing without any fault, when others are allowed to stay on would be a case of breach of fundamental right of equal protection given by this Constitution.
Precedent	Must an affected person in such a case ask the Judge to write down his order, so that he may appeal against it?
RatioOfTheDecision	or is he expected to ask for special leave from this <ORG>?
RatioOfTheDecision	If a <ORG> judge in England acted improperly, there may be no remedy because of the limitations on the rights of the subject against the Crown.
RatioOfTheDecision	But in such circumstances in England the hearing is considered vitiated and the decision voidable.
RatioOfTheDecision	This need not arise here.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> in our country in similar circumstances is not immune because there is a remedy to move this court for a writ against discriminatory treatment and this <ORG> should not in a suitable case shirk to issue a writ to a <ORG> Judge, who ignores the fundamental rights and his obligations under the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Other cases can easily be imagined under Article 14 , 15 , 19 , 20 , 21 and 22 of the Consti tution, in which there may be action by a Judge which may offend the fundamental rights and in which an appeal to this <ORG> will not only be not practicable but also quite an ineffective remedy.
RatioOfTheDecision	We need not be dismayed that the view I take means a slur on the High <ORG>s or that this <ORG> will be flooded with petitions under Article 32 of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Although the High <ORG>s possess a power to interfere by way of high prerogative writs of certiorari, mandamus and prohibition, such powers have not been invoked against the normal and routine work of subordinate courts and tribunals.
RatioOfTheDecision	The reason is that people understand the difference between an approach to the High <ORG> by way of appeals etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	and approach for the purpose of asking for writs under Article  226.
RatioOfTheDecision	Nor have the High <ORG>s spread a Procrustean bed for high prerogative writs for all actions to lie.
RatioOfTheDecision	Decisions of the courts have been subjected to statutory appeals and revisions but the losing side has not charged the Judge with a breach of fu ndamental rights because he ordered attachment of property belonging to a stranger to the litigation or by his order affected rights of the parties or even strangers.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is because the people understand the difference between normal proceedings of a civil nature and proceedings in which there is a breach of fundamental rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	The courts acts, between parties and even between parties and strangers, done impersonally and objectively are challengeable under the ordinary law only.
RatioOfTheDecision	But acts which involve the court with a fundamental right are quite different."
RatioOfTheDecision	One more passage from the judgment needs to be quoted.
RatioOfTheDecision	Observed the learned Judge:  "I may dispose of a few results which it was suggested, might flow from my view that this Court can issue a high prerogative writ to <ORG> for enforcement of fundamental rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was suggested that <ORG>s might issue writs to this Court and to other <ORG> and one Judge or Bench in <ORG> and <ORG> might issue a writ to another  judge or Bench in the same Court.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is an erroneous assumption.
RatioOfTheDecision	To begin with <ORG> cannot issue a writ to <ORG> because the writ goes down and not up.
RatioOfTheDecision	Similarly, <ORG> cannot issue a writ to <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The writ does not go to a court placed on an equal footing in the matter of jurisdiction XX XX XX I must hold that this English practice of not issuing writs in the same court is in the very nature of things.
RatioOfTheDecision	One <ORG> will thus not be able to issue a writ to <ORG> nor even to a court exercising the powers of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	In so far as this Court is concerned, the argument that one Bench or one Judge might issue a writ to another Bench or Judge, need hardly be considered.
RatioOfTheDecision	My opinion gives no support to such a view and I hope I have said nothing to give countenance to it.
Precedent	These are imaginary fears which have no reality either in law or in fact."

Precedent	The effect of the dicta in that case is to reduce the difference between jurisdictional error and error of law within jurisdiction almost to vanishing point.

RatioOfTheDecision	This comes perilously close to saying that there is jurisdiction if the decision is right in law but none if it is wrong.
RatioOfTheDecision	Almost any misconstruction of a statute can be represented as "basing their decision on a matter with which they have no right to deal", "impose an unwarranted condition" or "addressing themselves to a wrong question."
RatioOfTheDecision	The majority opinion in the case leaves a <ORG> or <ORG> with virtually no margin of legal error.
