RatioOfTheDecision	In dealing with this contention, one important aspect of the concept of jurisdiction has to be borne in mind.
RatioOfTheDecision	As pointed out by <NAME> in <NAME> v. <NAME>, (supra), the word "jurisdiction is a verbal <NAME> of many colours.".
RatioOfTheDecision	It is used in a wide and broad sense while dealing with administrative or quasi-judicial tribunals and subordinate courts over which the superior courts exercise a power of judicial review and superintendence.
RatioOfTheDecision	Then it is only a question of "how much latitude the court is prepared to allow" and "there is no yardstick to determine the magnitude of the error other than the opinion of the court."
RatioOfTheDecision	But the position is different wi th superior courts with unlimited jurisdiction.
RatioOfTheDecision	These are always presumed to act with jurisdiction and unless it is clearly shown that any particular order is patently one which could not, on any conceivable view of its jurisdiction, have been passed by such court, such an order can neither be ignored nor even recalled, annulled, revoked or set aside in subsequent proceedings by the same court.
Precedent	This distinction is well brought out in the speeches of Lord <NAME>, Lord Edmund- Devier and Lord <NAME> in <ORG> [1980] 2 A.E.R.
Precedent	634.
Precedent	In the interests of brevity, I resist the temptation to quote extracts from the speeches here.
Precedent	45.
Precedent	In the present case, the order passed is not one of patent lack of jurisdiction, as I shall explain later.
Precedent	Though I have come to the conclusion, on considering the arguments addressed now before us, that the direction in the order dated 16.2.1984 cannot be justified by reference to Article 142 of the Constitution or S. 407 of the 1973 Cr.
Precedent	P.C., that is not an in controvertible position.
Precedent	It was possible for another court to give a wider interpretation to these provisions and come to the conclusion that such an order could be made under those provisions.
Precedent	If this <ORG> had discussed the relevant provisions and specifically expressed such a conclusion, it could not have been modified in subsequent proceedings by this <ORG> merely because we are inclined to hold differently.
Precedent	The mere fact that the direction was given, without an elaborate discussion, cannot render it vulnerable to such review.
Precedent	46.
Precedent	<NAME> then placed considerable reliance on the observations of <ORG> in <NAME> v. <NAME>, [1984] 3 A.E.R.140 an appeal from a decision of the <ORG> of Appeal of St. Vincent and the Grenadines.
Precedent	Briefly the facts were that <NAME> had obtained an interim injunction against <NAME> and two others on 31.5.1979 which the latter refused to obey.
Precedent	The respondents motion for committal of the appellant for contempt was dismissed by the High <ORG> of Saint Vincent.
Fact	<ORG> allowed the respondents application; the appellants were found to be in contempt and also asked to pay respondents costs.
RatioOfTheDecision	However, no penalty was inflicted because the appellant would have been entitled to succeed on an application for setting aside the injunction, has he filed one.
RatioOfTheDecision	The main attack by the appellant on <ORG> judgment was based on the contention that, as a consequence of the operation of certain rules of <ORG>, the interlocutory injunction granted by <ORG> was a nullity: so disobedience to it could not constitute a contempt of court.
Precedent	Lord <NAME> observed:  <ORG> accepted this contention, the Court of Appeal rejected it, in their Lordships' view correctly, on the short and well established ground that an order made by a court of unlimited jurisdiction, such as <ORG> of Saint Vincent must be obeyed unless and until it has been set aside by the court.
Precedent	For this proposition <ORG> cited the passage in the j udgment of <NAME> in <NAME> v. <NAME>, [1952] 2 All.
Precedent	E.R.
Precedent	567 at 569, (1952) P. 285 at 288.
Precedent	It is the plain and unqualified obligation of every person against, or in respect of whom an order is made by <ORG> to obey it unless and until that order is discharged.
Precedent	The uncompromising nature of this obligation is shown by the fact that it extends even to cases where the person affected by an order believes it to be irregular or even void.
Precedent	Lord <NAME>, Leven to cases where the person affected by an order believes it to be irregular or even void.
Precedent	Lord <NAME>, L.C.
Precedent	said in <NAME> v. <NAME>, [1946] 1 Coop Temp Cott 338 at 342, 47 E.R.884 at 855: "A party, who knows of an order, whether null or valid, regular or irregular, cannot be permitted to disobey it ..
Precedent	It would be most dangerous to hold that the suitors, or their solicitors, could themselves judge whether an order was null or valid-whether it was regular or irregular.
Precedent	That they should come to the court and not take up on themselves to determine such a question.
Precedent	That the course of a party knowing of an order, which was null or irregular, and who might be affected by it, was plain.
Precedent	He should apply to the <ORG> that it might be discharged.
Precedent	As long as it existed it must not be obeyed."
Precedent	Such being the nature of this obligation, two consequences will, in general, follow from its breach.
Precedent	The first is that anyone who dis-  160  obeys an order of the court.....is in contempt and may be published by committal or attachment or otherwise.
Precedent	It is in their Lordships view, says all that needs to be said on this topic.
Precedent	It is not itself sufficient reason for dismissing this appeal.
Precedent	Having said this, the learned Law Lord proceeded to say: "The cases that are referred to in these dicta do not support the proposition that there is any category of orders of a court of unlimited jurisdiction of this kind, what they do support is the quite different proposition that there is a category of orders of such a court which a person affected by the or <ORG> is entitled to apply to have set aside ex debito justitiae in the exercise of the inherent jurisdiction of the court without his needing to have recourse to the rules that deals expressly with proceedings to set aside or<ORG>s for irregularity and give to the Judge a discretion as to the or<ORG> he will make.
Precedent	The judges in the case that have drawn the distinction between the two types of or<ORG>s have cautiously refrained from seeking to lay down a comprehensive definition of defects that bring an or<ORG> in the category that attracts ex debito justitiae the right to have it set aside save that specifically it includes or<ORG>s that have been obtained in breach of rules of natural justice.
Precedent	The contrasting legal concepts of voidness and voidability form part of the English law of contract.
Precedent	They are inapplicable to or<ORG>s made by a court of unlimited jurisdiction in the course of contentions litigation.
Precedent	Such an or<ORG> is either irregular or regular.
Precedent	If it is irregular it can be set aside by the court that made it on a pplication to that court, if it is regular it can only be set aside by an appellate court on appeal if there is one to which an appeal lies."
Precedent	<NAME> strongly relied on this passage and, modifying his earlier, somewhat extreme, contention that the direction given on 16.2.1984 being a nullity and without jurisdiction could be ignored by all concerned-even by the trial judge-he contended, on the strength of these observations, that he was at least entitled ex debito justitiae to come to this <ORG> and request the court, in the interests of justice, to set aside the earlier order "without his needing to have recourse to the rules that deal expressly with proceedings to set aside orders for irre gularity", if only on the ground that the order had been made in breach of the principles of natural justice.
Precedent	Violation of the principles of natural justice, he contended, renders the direction a nullity without any further proof of prejudice (see <NAME> v. <NAME>, [1981] 1 S.C.R.
Precedent	746 at 766) .
Precedent	47.
Precedent	Learned counsel cont ended, in this context, that the fact the direction had been given in the earlier proceedings in this very case need not stand in the way of our giving relief, if we are really satisfied that the direction had been issued per incuriam, without complying with the principles of natural justice and purported to confer a jurisdiction on <ORG> which it did not possess.
Precedent	In this context he relied on certain decisions holding that an erroneous decision on a point of jurisdiction will not constitute res judicata.
Precedent	<CITATION_1006709> ,  3 S.C.R.
Precedent	830, this <ORG> observed:  "A question relating to the jurisdiction of a <ORG> cannot be deemed to have been finally determined by an erroneous decision of the <ORG>.
Precedent	If by an erroneous interpretation of the statute, the <ORG> holds that it has no jurisdiction, the question would not, in our judgment, operate as res judicata.
Precedent	Similarly, by an erroneous decision, if the <ORG> assumes jurisdiction which it does not possess under the statute, the question cannot operate as  res judicata between the same parties, whether the cause of action in the subsequent litigation is the same or otherwise.
Precedent	It is true that in determining the application of the rule of res judicata the <ORG> is not concerned with the correctness or otherwise of the earlier judgment.
Precedent	The matter in issue, if it is one purely of fact, decided in the earlier proceeding by a competent court must in a subsequent litigation between the same parties be regarded as finally decided and cannot be re-opened.
Precedent	A mixed question of law and fact determined in the earlier proceeding between the same parties may not, for the same reason, be questioned in a subsequent proceeding between the same parties.
Precedent	0 xxxxx xxxxx Where, however the question is one purely of law and it relates to the jurisdiction of the <ORG> or a decision of the court sanctioning something which is illegal, by resor to the rule of res judicata a party affected by the decision will not be precluded from challenging the validity of that order under the rule  of res judicata, for a rule of procedure cannot supersede the law of the land. "
Precedent	Counsel also relied on the decision of this Court in <CITATION_924262> ,  2 S.C.C.271, where it was held that the principle of constructive res judicata was not applicable to habeas corpus proceedings.
Precedent	He also referred to the observations of <NAME> in <CITATION_351056> , AIR 1972 Guj.
Precedent	148 that no act of the court or irregularity can come in the way of justice being done and one of the highest and the first duty of all courts is to take care that the act of the court does no injury to the suitors.
Precedent	He also made reference to the maxim that an act of, or mistake on the part, of a court shall cause prejudice to no one, vide: Jang Singh v. Brij Lal,  2 S.C.R.
Precedent	145 at p. 159.
Argument	Relying on these decisions and passages from various treatises which I do not consider it necessary to set out in in extenso here, <NAME> contended that this court should not consider itself bound by the earlier order of the <ORG> or any kind of technicalit y where the liberty of an individual and the rights guaranteed to him under Articles 14 and 21 of the Constitution are in issue.
Argument	It is urged that, if this Court agrees with him that the direction dated 16.2.1984 was an illegal one, this Court should not hesitate nay, it should hasten-to set aside the said order and repair the injustice done to the appellant without further delay.
Precedent	On the other hand, Sri <NAME> vehemently urged that the present attempt to have the entire matter reopened constitutes a gross abuse of the process of court, that it is well settled that the principle of res judicata is also available in criminal matters (vide <CITATION_56785029> ,  2 S.C.C.
Precedent	466 and <CITATION_119752> ,  S.C.c.
Precedent	Crl.
Precedent	774) that in the United States the principle of res judicata governs even jurisdictional issues and that "the slightest hospitality to the accused's pleas will lead to a grave miscarriage of justice and set up a precedent perilous to public interest.
Precedent	48.
Precedent	I have given careful thought to these contentio ns.
Precedent	The appellant's counsel has relied to a considerable extent on the maxim "actus curiae neminem gravabit" for contending that it is not only within the power, but a duty as well, of this <ORG> to correct its own mistakes in order to see that no party is prejudiced by a mistake of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	I am not persuaded that the earlier decision could be reviewed on the application of the said maxim.
Precedent	I share the view of my learned brother <NAME>, J. that this maxim has very limited application and that it cannot be availed of to correct or review specific conclusions arrived at in a judicial decision.
Precedent	My.
Argument	brother <NAME>, J. has further taken the view that this <ORG> cannot exercise any inherent powers for setting right any injustice that may have been caused as a result of an earlier order of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	While alive to the consideration that "the highest court in the land should not, by technicalities of procedure, forge fetters on its own feet and disable itself in cases of serious miscarriages of jus tice", he has, nevertheless, come to the conclusion that "the remedy of the appellant, if any, is by recourse to article 137 and nowhere else."
RatioOfTheDecision	It is at this point that I would record a dissent from his opinion.
RatioOfTheDecision	In my view, the decisions cited do indicate that situations can and do arise where this <ORG> may be constrained to recall or modify an order which has been passed by it earlier and that when ex facie there is something radically wrong with the earlier order, this <ORG> may have to exercise its plenary and inherent powers to recall the earlier order without considering itself bound by the nice technicalities of the procedure for getting this done.
RatioOfTheDecision	Where a mistake is committed by a subordinate court or a High <ORG>, there are ample powers in this <ORG> to remedy the situation.
RatioOfTheDecision	But where the mistake is in an earlier order of this <ORG>, there is no way of having it corrected except by approaching this <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Sometimes, the remedy sought can be brought within the four comers of the procedural law in whic h event there can be no hurdle in the way of achieving the desired result.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the mere fact that, for some reason, the conventional remedies are not available should not, in my view, render this <ORG> powerless to give relief.
RatioOfTheDecision	As pointed out by Lord <NAME> in <NAME> v. <NAME>, [ 19841 3 A.E.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	140, it may not be possible or prudent to lay down a comprehensive list of defects that will attract the ex debito justitiae relief.
RatioOfTheDecision	Suffice it to say that the court can grant relief where there is some manifest illegality or want of jurisdiction in the earlier order or some palpable injustice is shown to have resulted.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such a power can be traced either to article 142 of the Constitution or to the powers inherent in this <ORG> as the apex court and the guardian of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	49.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is, however, indisputable that such power has to be exercised in the "rarest of rare" cases.
RatioOfTheDecision	As rightly pointed out by <NAME>, there is great need for judicial discipline of the highest order in exercising such a power, as  any laxity in this regard may not only impair the eminence, dignity and integrity of this <ORG> but may also lead to chaotic consequences.
RatioOfTheDecision	Nothing should be done to create an impression that this <ORG> can be easily persuaded to alter its views on any matter and that a larger Bench of the <ORG> will not only be able to reverse the precedential effect of an earlier ruling but may also be inclined to go back on it and render it ineffective in its application and binding nature even in regard to subsequent proceedings in the same case.
RatioOfTheDecision	<CITATION_608874> .,  2 S.C.R.
Precedent	603, this <ORG> held that it had the power, in appropriate cases, to reconsider a previous decision given by it.
Precedent	While concurring in this conclusion, <NAME> sounded a note of warning of consequences which is more germane in the present context:  "The question then arises as to the principles on which and the limits within which this power should be exercised.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is of course not possible to enumerate them exhaustively, nor is it even  desirable that they should not crystallised into rigid and inflexible rules.
RatioOfTheDecision	But one principle stands out prominently above the rest, and that is that in general, there should be finality in the decisions of the highest courts in the land, and that is for the benefit and protection of the public.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this connection, it is necessary to bear in mind that next to legislative enactments, it is decisions of <ORG> that form the most important source of law.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is on the faith of decisions that rights are acquired and obligations incurred, and States and subjects alike shape their course of action.
RatioOfTheDecision	It must greatly impair the value of the decisions of this <ORG>, if the notion came to be entertained that there was nothing certain or final about them, which must be the consequence if the points decided therein came to be re-considered on the merits every time they were raised.
RatioOfTheDecision	It should be noted that though <ORG> has repeatedly declared that it has the power to reconsider its decisions, in fact, no instan ce has been quoted in which it did actually reverse its previous decision except in ecclesiastical cases.
RatioOfTheDecision	If that is the correct position, then the power to reconsider is one which should be exercised very sparingly and only in exceptiona1 circumstances, such as when a material provision of law had been overlooked, or where a fundamental assumption on which the decision is base(1 turns out to be mistaken.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the present case, it is not suggested that in deciding the question of law as they did in <CITATION_424874> .,  S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	l069 the learned Judges ignored any material provisions of law, or were under any misapprehension as to a matter fundamental to the decision.
RatioOfTheDecision	The arguments for the appellant before us were in fact only a repetition of the very contentions which were urged before the learned Judges and negatived by them.
RatioOfTheDecision	The question then resolves itself to this.
RatioOfTheDecision	Can we differ from a previous decision of this <ORG>, because a view contrary to the one taken therein appears to be preferable?
RatioOfTheDecision	I would unhesita tingly answer it in the negative, not because the view previously taken must necessarily be infallible but because it is important in public interest that the law declared should be certain and final rather than that it should be declared in one sense.
RatioOfTheDecision	Or the other.
RatioOfTheDecision	That, I conceive, in the reason behind article 141.
RatioOfTheDecision	There are questions of law on which it is not possible to avoid difference of opinion, and the present case is itself a signal example of it.
RatioOfTheDecision	The object of article 141 is that the decisions of this <ORG> on these questions should settle the controversy, and that they should be followed as law by all the <ORG>s, and if they are allowed to be reopened because a different view appears to be the better one, then the very purpose with which article 141 has been enacted will be defeated, and the prospect will have been opened of litigants subjecting our decisions to a continuous process of attack before successive Benches in the hope that with changes in the personnel of the <ORG> which time must inevi tably bring, a different view might find acceptance.
RatioOfTheDecision	I can imagine nothing more damaging to the prestige of this <ORG> or to the value of its pronouncements.
Precedent	In <NAME> v. <NAME>, 18 C.L.R.54, it was observed that a question settled by a previous decision should not be allowed to be reopened "upon a mere suggestion that some or all of the Members of the later <ORG> might arrive at a different conclusion if the matter was res integra.
Precedent	Otherwise, there would be grave danger of want of continuity in the interpretation of the law" (per <NAME>, C.J.
Precedent	at p. 58).
Precedent	It is for this reason that article 141 invests decisions of this <ORG> with special authority, but the weight of that authority can only be what we ourselves give to it."
Precedent	Even in the context of a power of review, properly so called, Ven- <NAME>, J. had this to say in <CITATION_996328> .,  1 S.C.C.
Precedent	288:  "The review petition was admitted after the appeal had been dismissed only because Nandini Satpathy cases, (1987 1 S.C.C.269 and 1987 lS.C.C.279)  had been subsequently referred to a larger bench to review the earlier decisions.
Precedent	When the earlier decisions are allowed to remain intact, there is no justification to reverse the decision of this <ORG> by which the appeal had already been dismissed.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is no warrant for this extraordinary procedure to be adopted in this case.
RatioOfTheDecision	The reversal of the earlier judgment of this <ORG> by this process strikes at the finally of judgments of this <ORG> and would amount to the abuse of the power of review vested in this <ORG>, particularly in a criminal case.
RatioOfTheDecision	It may be noted that no other court in the country has been given the power of review in criminal cases.
Precedent	I am of the view that the majority judgment of <NAME> and <NAME>, JJ.
Precedent	should remain undisturbed.
Precedent	This case cannot be converted into an apeal against the earlier decision of this <ORG> "  The attempt of the appellant here is more far-reaching.
Precedent	He seeks not the mere upsetting of a precedent of this <ORG> nor the upsetting of a decision of <ORG> t or this <ORG> in accordance with the normal procedure.
Precedent	What he wants from us is a declaration that an order passed by a five judge <ORG> is wrong and that it should, in effect, be annulled by us.
Precedent	This should not be done, in my view, unless the earlier order is vitiated by a patent lack of jurisdiction or has resulted in grave injustice or has clearly abridged the fundamental rights of the appellant.
Precedent	The question that arises is whether the present case can be brought within the narrow range of exceptions which calls for such interference.
Precedent	I am inclined to think that it does not.
Precedent	50.
Precedent	I have indicated earlier, while discussing the contentions urged by <NAME> that some of them were plausible and, that, if I were asked to answer these questions posed by counsel for the first time, I might agree with his answers.
RatioOfTheDecision	But I have also indicated that, in my view, they do not constitute the only way of answering the questions posed by the learned counsel.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus, to the question: did this <ORG> have the jurisdictio n to issue the impugned direction, a plausible answer could well be that it did, if one remembers that one of the transferred cases before this <ORG> was the revision petition before <ORG> in which a transfer of the case to <ORG> has been asked for and if one gives a wide interpretation to the provisions of Article 142 of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	On the question whether this <ORG> could transfer the case to a High <ORG> Judge, who was not a Special Judge, a court could certainly accept the view urged by <NAME> that s. 7(1) of the 1952 Act should not be so construed as to exclude the application of the procedural provisions of the Cr.P.C .
RatioOfTheDecision	in preference to the view that has found favour with me.
RatioOfTheDecision	Though the order dated 16.2.1984 contains no reference to, or discussion of, S. 407 ., this line of thinking of the judges who issued the direction does surface in their observations in their decision of even date rendered on the complainant's special leave petition,  2 S.C.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	914 at page  943-4.I have already pointed out that, if the transfer is referable to s. 407 of the 1973 Cr.P.C ., it cannot be impugned as offending Article 14 and 21 of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The mere fact that the judges did not discuss at length the facts or the provisions of s. 407 .
RatioOfTheDecision	vis-a-vis the 1952 Act or give a reasoned order as to why they thought that the trial should be in <ORG> itself cannot render their direction susceptible to a charge of discrimination.
RatioOfTheDecision	A view can certainly be taken that the mere entrustment of this case to <ORG> for trial does not perpetrate manifest or grave injustice.
RatioOfTheDecision	On the other hand, prima facie, it is something beneficial to the accused and equitable in the interest of justice.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such trial by <ORG>, in the first instance, will be the rule in cases where a criminal trial is withdrawn to <ORG> under s. 407 of the .
RatioOfTheDecision	Or where a <ORG> judge has been constituted as a Special Judge either under the 1952 Act or some other statute.
RatioOfTheDecision	The absence of an appeal t o <ORG> with a right of seeking for further leave to appeal to <ORG> may be considered outweighed by the consideration that the original trial will be in <ORG> (as in <NAME> cases of old, in the Presidency Towns) with a statutory right of appeal to <ORG> under s. 374 of the .
RatioOfTheDecision	In this situation, it is difficult to say that the direction issued by this <ORG> in the impugned order is based on a view which is manifestly incorrect, palpably absurd or patently without jurisdiction.
RatioOfTheDecision	Whether it will be considered right or wrong by a different <ORG> having a second-look at the issues is a totally different thing.
RatioOfTheDecision	It will be agreed on all hands that it will not behove the prestige and glory of this <ORG> as envisaged under the Constitution if earlier decisions are revised or recalled solely because a later <ORG> takes a different view of the issues involved.
RatioOfTheDecision	Granting that the power of review is available, it is one to be sparingly exercised only in extraordinary or emergent s ituations when there can be no two opinion about the error or lack of jurisdiction in the earlier order and there are adequate reasons to invoke a resort to an unconventional method of recalling or revoking the same.
RatioOfTheDecision	In my opinion, such a situation is not present here.
RatioOfTheDecision	51.
RatioOfTheDecision	The only question that has been bothering me is that the appellant had been given no chance of being heard before the impugned direction was given and one cannot say whether <ORG> would have acted in the same way even if he had been given such opportunity.
RatioOfTheDecision	However, in the circumstances of the case, I have come to the conclusion that this is not a fit case to interfere with the earlier order on that ground.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is true that the audi altarem partem rule is a basic requirement of the rule of law.
RatioOfTheDecision	But judicial decisions also show that the degree of compliance with this rule and the extent of consequences flowing from failure to do so will vary from case to case.
Precedent	<NAME>, J.
Precedent	Observed thus in <CITATION_1512746> , 3 S.C.R.
Precedent	4/7 thus:  "a n order which infringed a fundamental freedom passed in violation of the audi alteram partem rule was a nullity.
Precedent	A determination is no determination if it is contrary to the constitutional mandate of Art.
Precedent	19.
Precedent	On this footing the externment order was of no effect and its violation was not offence.
Precedent	Any order made without hearing the party affected is void and ineffectual to bind parties from the beginng if the injury is to a constitutionally guaranteed right.
Precedent	May be that in ordinary legislation or at common law a <ORG> having jurisdiction and failing to-hear the parties may commit an illegality which may render the proceedings voidable when a direct attack was made thereon by way of appeal, revision or review but nullity is the consequence of unconstitutionality and so the order of an administrative authority charged with the duty of complying with natural justice in the exercise of power before restricting the fundamental right of a citizen is void ab initio and of no legal efficacy.
Precedent	The duty to hear menacl es his jurisdictional exercise and any act is, in its inception, void except when performed in accordance with the conditions laid down in regard to hearing. "
RatioOfTheDecision	(emphasis added) So far as this case is concerned, I have indicated earlier that the direction Of 16.2.1984 cannot be said to have infringed the fundamental rights of the appellant or caused any miscarriage of justice.
RatioOfTheDecision	As pointed out by <NAME>, the appellant did know, on 16.2.84, that the judges were giving such a direction and yet he did not protest.
RatioOfTheDecision	Perhaps he did think that being tried by a <ORG> Judge would be more beneficial to him, as indeed was likely to be.
RatioOfTheDecision	That apart, as discussed earlier, several opportunities were available for the appellant to set this right.
RatioOfTheDecision	He did not move his little finger to obtain a variation of this direction from this Court.
RatioOfTheDecision	He is approaching the Court nearly after two years of his trial by the learned judge in the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Volumes of testimony, we are told, have been recorded and numerous exhibits ha ve been admitted as evidence.
RatioOfTheDecision	Though the trial is only at the stage of the framing charges, the trial being according to the warrant procedure, a lot of evidence has already gone in and the result of the conclusions of <NAME>, J. would be to wipe the slate clean.
RatioOfTheDecision	To take the entire matter back at this stage to square no.
RatioOfTheDecision	1 would be the very negation of the purpose of the 1952 Act to speed up all such trials and would result in more injustice than justice from an objective point of view.
RatioOfTheDecision	As pointed out by Lord <NAME> in <CITATION_514007> for the Home Departrnent ex parte Mughal, l 19731 3 All E.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	796, the rules of natural justice must not be stretched too far.
RatioOfTheDecision	They should not be allowed to be exploited as a purely technical weapon to undo a decision which does not in reality cause substantial injustice and which, had the party been really aggrieved thereby, could have been set right by immediate action.
RatioOfTheDecision	After giving my best anxious and deep thought to the pros and cons of the situation I have co me to the conclusion that this is not one of those cases in which I would consider it appropriate to recall the earlier direction and order a retrial of the appellant de novo before a Special Judge.
RulingByPresentCourt	I would, therefore, dismiss the appeal.
